Testimony of Zina with an Absent Woman
Ruling on establishing zina by testimony when the woman is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the hadd for zina when the woman is absent, compare it with theft, and explain the concept of 'doubt of a doubt'.

Core Idea
Absence of the woman in zina does not block the man's hadd, unlike theft where the victim's claim is a condition.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on testimony of zina when the woman named as the partner is absent from court. The ruling is clear: the man receives the hadd penalty. The passage contrasts this with theft, where the victim's presence is necessary, and addresses a potential objection about the woman possibly claiming marriage, which would create a doubt. Understanding this distinction requires grasping the Hanafi concept of 'shubhat al-shubha' (doubt of a doubt), which is too weak to block the penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its evidence
The passage states: if witnesses establish zina with an absent woman, the man is punished with hadd. The evidence includes the Prophet's stoning of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyya when they confessed to zina with absent partners. Identify the act: zina proven by testimony. The ruling: hadd wajib on the man.
The hadd is implemented without requiring the woman's presence.
2
Contrast with theft
The passage explicitly contrasts: in theft, if the victim is absent, the hadd (amputation) is not imposed. Why? Because the victim's claim is a condition (shart) for theft, but not for zina. In zina, the crime is established solely by four witnesses, regardless of the partner's presence.
This contrast highlights that the requirement of a claim (da'wa) differs between hudud crimes.
3
Address the shubha objection
A hypothetical objection: why not require the woman's presence in zina too, since she might claim marriage (a shubha)? The Hanafi response: a claim of marriage is a direct shubha that can block hadd, but the mere possibility of such a claim is only a 'shubhat al-shubha' (doubt of a doubt), which is too weak to block. Considering it would close the door of hudud.
The distinction between a direct doubt and a doubt of a doubt is crucial in Hanafi penal theory.
4
Distinguish from qisas
The passage further clarifies that this reasoning does not conflict with qisas: if two partners are entitled to qisas and one is absent, the present partner cannot execute because the absent one might forgive. Forgiveness is a real possibility (a doubt of the remover), not a doubt of a doubt.
This distinction reinforces the principle that only a genuine, plausible doubt, not a remote possibility, can block a hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أثبتوا زناه بغائبة حد، بخلاف السرقة ولو أقر بالزنا بمجهولة حد، وإن أي الشهود على رجل شهدوا بذلك لا، كاختلافهم في طوعها، أي الشهود على رجل ولو أثبتوا زناه إلخ أي إذا شهدوا على رجل أنه زنى بفلانة وهي غائبة عن مجلس القاضي وهم يعرفونها حد الرجل بإجماع الأربعة وكذا لو أقر بالزنا بغائبة يقام عليه الحد بالإجماع؛ لأنه عليه رجم ماعزاً والغامدية حين أقرا بالزنا بغائبين. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٨٦/١] (فتح) بخلاف السرقه: أي إذا شهدت الشهود أنه سرق من فلان الغائب لا يجب الحد فلا يقطع يده؛ لأن بالغيبة تفوت الدعوى، وهي شرط في السرقة دون الزنا، فإن قلت: ينبغي أن لا يحد في الزنا أيضاً حتى يحضر الغائب؛ لاحتمال أن يدعي النكاح فيكون شبهة قلت: دعوى النكاح شبهة؛ لاحتمال الصدق فتعتبر، واحتمال الدعوى شبهة الشبهة فلا تعتبر ؛ لأن اعتبارها يؤدي إلى سد باب الحدود ولا ينتقض هذا بالقصاص إذا كان بين شريكين، وكان أحدهما غائباً لا يتمكن الحاضر من الاستيفاء؛ لاحتمال العفو من الغائب؛ لأن العفو حقيقة المسقط، واحتماله يكون شبهة المسقط لا شبهة الشبهة. (عيني، فتح)
If they establish his zina with an absent woman, the hadd is imposed, unlike theft. And if he confesses to zina with an unknown woman, the hadd is imposed. And if the witnesses testify regarding that no, like their disagreement regarding her consent. [Commentary]: 'If they establish his zina...' i.e. if they testify against a man that he committed zina with a certain woman, and she is absent from the judge's session, and they know her, the man is punished with the hadd by consensus of the four [caliphs? or imams?]. Similarly, if he confesses to zina with an absent woman, the hadd is established by consensus because the Prophet stoned Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyya when they confessed to zina with absent partners. (Fath) 'Unlike theft': i.e. if the witnesses testify that he stole from an absent person, the hadd is not imposed, so his hand is not amputated because absence eliminates the claim, which is a condition in theft but not in zina. If you say: It would be appropriate not to impose the hadd in zina either until the absent woman is present, because she might claim marriage, which would be a doubt. I say: The claim of marriage is a doubt because it may be true, so it is considered. But the mere possibility of a claim is a doubt of a doubt, so it is not considered, because considering it would lead to closing the door of hudud. And this is not contradicted by the case of qisas between two partners where one is absent, as the present partner cannot execute the qisas due to the possibility that the absent one might forgive, because forgiveness is a real remover, and its possibility is a doubt of the remover, not a doubt of a doubt. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where witnesses testify that a man committed zina with a specific woman who is absent from court. The ruling is clear: the man receives the hadd penalty. The reason given is that unlike theft, zina does not require the victim to be present to file a claim; the crime is established by the testimony of four witnesses. The text also notes that if the man confesses to zina with an absent woman, the hadd is also imposed, citing the Prophet's stoning of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyya when they confessed to zina with absent partners. The passage then contrasts this with theft: if witnesses testify that a man stole from an absent person, the hadd of amputation is not imposed because the victim's claim is a condition for theft, and absence prevents it. A potential objection is raised: why not require the woman's presence in zina too, since she might claim marriage and thus create a doubt? The Hanafi response is that the likelihood of a mere claim is too remote—it is a 'doubt of a doubt'—and considering it would effectively block all hudud. This is distinguished from qisas where a partner's absence prevents execution because forgiveness from the absent partner is a real possibility.

Key Terms
بغائبة (bigha'iba): The woman with whom zina is committed is absent from the court session
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that can prevent the hadd penalty, such as a claim of marriage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because theft requires the victim’s claim as a condition, unlike zina
B) Because zina is a more serious sin than theft
C) Because the woman’s absence in zina is always a valid excuse
D) Because theft requires two witnesses whereas zina requires four

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the hadd for zina is imposed on the man regardless of the woman's absence, and only a plausible direct doubt can block it.

Confession of Zina with an Unknown Woman: Hadd and the Role of Knowledge
Ruling with proof and distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for zina by confession and by testimony when the woman is unknown, and explain the reason for the difference based on knowledge and presumption.

Core Idea
The confessor knows his own women; witnesses do not — so hadd follows knowledge.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a key distinction in Hanafi hudud jurisprudence: the difference between a confession and testimony when the woman involved in zina is unknown. The ruling hinges on who lacks knowledge — the confessor or the witnesses — and the strength of presumption (zahir) regarding a Muslim's actions. Understanding this distinction clarifies how the Hanafi school balances textual evidence with practical presumptions to avoid closing the door of hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Separate the case where a man confesses to zina with an unknown woman from the case where witnesses testify against a man for zina with an unknown woman.
The passage explicitly contrasts these two.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For confession: the hadd is imposed on the confessor. For testimony: the accused is not punished.
Confession leads to hadd; testimony does not lead to hadd.
3
Extract the rationale (ta'lil)
The confessor cannot claim ignorance of his own wife or slave women, so his lack of knowledge indicates zina. For witnesses, the presumption (zahir) is that a Muslim man would not commit zina openly, so the woman could be his wife or slave.
The passage says: 'لأنه لا يخفى عليه امرأته ولا أمته' and 'لأن المسلم يمنعه دينه من ارتكاب المحرم ظاهراً'.
4
Evaluate the rejection of remote possibilities
The passage rejects considering far-fetched possibilities (e.g., the woman being an unknown slave through inheritance) because that would block all hudud.
This is a Hanafi principle: remote probabilities (ihtimal ba'id) are not given weight in hudud.
5
Note the cross-madhhab implication
The Shafi'i school may consider the possibility of a valid relationship even in the confessor's case, potentially dropping the hadd. The Hanafi view is based on the strong presumption against ignorance of one's own women.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد: أي إذا أقر رجل أنه زنى بامرأة لا يعرفها حد المقر؛ لأنه لا يخفى عليه امرأته ولا أمته خصوصاً إذا لم يكن له امرأة ولا أمة ولو كانت تعرفها ولا يعتبر الاحتمال البعيد بأن كانت أمته بجهة من الجهات كالإرث، وهو لا يعرف ذلك، أو بالتوالد من مملوكاته أو من مملوكات آبائه؛ لأن اعتبار الاحتمال البعيد موهوم فلا يعتبر؛ ولأن ذلك يؤدي إلى انسداد باب الحدود، وإن شهدوا بذلك لا أي لو شهد الشهود أنه زنى بامرأة لا نعرفها لا يحد المشهود عليه؛ لأن المزني بها يحتمل أن تكون امرأته أو أمته، بل هو الظاهر؛ لأن المسلم يمنعه دينه من ارتكاب المحرم ظاهراً، ولا يلزم من عدم معرفة الشهود الموطوءة أن يكون زنا، بخلاف ما إذا لم يعرفها الزاني. رمز الحقائق ٣٨٦/١] (مستخلص)
Hadd: i.e., if a man confesses that he committed zina with a woman he does not know, the confessor is punished with the hadd, because his wife and his slave woman are not unknown to him, especially if he has no wife and no slave woman. If he knew her, he would know her status. Remote possibilities are not considered, such as that she might be his slave woman through some means like inheritance while he does not know that, or through offspring from his or his ancestors' slaves. Because considering such remote possibilities is fanciful and not taken into account, and because that would lead to closing the door of hudud. As for 'if they testify regarding that no' i.e. if the witnesses testify that he committed zina with a woman they do not know, the accused is not punished, because the woman might be his wife or his slave woman—indeed that is the apparent presumption, because the Muslim's religion prevents him from committing the prohibited openly. The witnesses' lack of knowledge of the woman does not necessarily mean zina occurred, unlike the case where the zani himself does not know her. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/386) (Mukhtalaf)
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage examines two scenarios involving knowledge of the woman in zina. First, if a man confesses that he committed zina with a woman he does not know, the hadd is imposed on him. The reasoning is that a man knows his own wife and slave women, so his lack of knowledge indicates the woman is neither, and the zina is established. Even if it is remotely possible that she could be his slave through unknown inheritance, such a far-fetched possibility is disregarded because considering it would block the enforcement of hudud entirely. Second, if witnesses testify that a man committed zina with a woman they do not know, the accused is not punished. The default assumption (zahir) is that a Muslim man would not commit zina openly, so the woman could be his wife or slave. The witnesses' ignorance does not prove zina. This contrasts with the first case where the confessor himself does not know the woman, because he would have known his own legal relations.

Key Terms
مجهولة (majhula): A woman unknown to the confessor or the witnesses

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hadd imposed on a man who confesses to zina with an unknown woman, but not when witnesses testify against a man for zina with an unknown woman?
A) Because the confessor is always truthful, while witnesses may be mistaken
B) Because the confessor knows his own wife/slave and cannot claim ignorance; witnesses cannot rule out that the woman is his wife
C) Because confession requires four witnesses to be valid, whereas testimony does not
D) Because the woman’s identity is irrelevant in zina cases

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between confession and testimony in zina: the confessor's ignorance of the woman is incriminating, but witnesses' ignorance gives the accused the benefit of the doubt.

Witness Disagreement on Consent in Zina: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on witness disagreement about consent in zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when witnesses disagree on consent in zina, explain the underlying reasoning, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Contradictory descriptions of the act invalidate the required four-witness agreement, so the hadd is dropped for both according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute. Four witnesses testify to zina, but two say the woman consented and two say she was coerced. The ruling hinges on whether the witnesses' agreement on the man's involvement is sufficient or whether the contradiction in the description of the act itself prevents the hadd. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the hadd is dropped for both parties because the act cannot have contradictory attributes, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the man is still liable. Let's dissect their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Four witnesses testify that a man committed zina with a woman, but they disagree on consent: two say taw' (consent) and two say ikrah (coercion). Determine the legal issue: does this disagreement affect the hadd?
2
State the Two Positions
Abu Hanifa (with Zufar): hadd dropped for both man and woman. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): man punished, woman not.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Proof
Zina is a single act performed by both parties. Consent and coercion are contradictory attributes; the same act cannot be both. The witnesses disagree on the essential description, so the subject of testimony differs. The required nisab (four witnesses agreeing on the same thing) is not met, so hadd drops for both.
Key phrase: 'الزنا فعل واحد يقوم بهما لا يتصف بوصفين متضادين'.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's Proof
All witnesses agree that the man committed zina. Their disagreement is only about the woman's consent. Therefore, the man's hadd is established. The woman goes free because the witnesses did not agree on her guilt.
They focus on the agreement regarding the man.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, both go free; under Sahibayn, the man is punished and the woman is not. Also note that if all four agree on coercion, the hadd applies only to the man; if all agree on consent, both are punished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شهدوا بذلك لا : أي إذا شهد أربعة أنه زنى بفلانة، واختلفوا في طوع ،المرأة، فقال اثنان منهم: بأنه زنى بها بطوعها، وقال الآخرون: بل استكرهها، سقط الحد عن الرجل والمرأة جميعاً عند أبي حنيفة، وهو قول زفر، وقالا: يحد الرجل خاصة؛ لأنهم اتفقوا في طوع الرجل، واختلفوا في طوع المرأة فلا تحد، بل يحد الرجل، ولأبي حنيفة: أن المشهود به قد اختلف؛ لأن الزنا فعل واحد يقوم بهما، وكل فعل يقوم بهما لا يتصف بوصفين متضادين، والشهود أثبتوا له وصفين متضادين؛ لأن الطوع يوجب اشتراكهما في الزنا، والكره يوجب انفراد الرجل به، واجتماع الوصفين المتضادين متعذر ، فكان كل واحد منهما خلاف الآخر، واختلف المشهود به، ولم يتم على كل واحد منهما نصاب الشهادة فلا يحد أحد. (عيني، فتح)
And if they testify regarding that no: i.e., if four testify that he committed zina with so-and-so, and they differ regarding the woman’s consent: two say he committed zina with her consent, and the others say rather he coerced her; the hadd is dropped from both the man and the woman according to Abu Hanifa, and this is the saying of Zufar. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the man is punished specifically, because they agreed on the consent of the man, and differed on the consent of the woman, so she is not punished, but the man is punished. For Abu Hanifa: the subject of testimony has differed, because zina is a single act performed by both, and any act done by both cannot be described by two contradictory attributes. The witnesses attributed to him two contradictory attributes, because consent requires their partnership in zina, while coercion requires the man to be alone. Combining two contradictory attributes is impossible, so each one contradicts the other, and the subject of testimony differs. Since the complete number of witnesses is not attained for each, no one is punished.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical dispute within the Hanafi school itself. The scenario: four witnesses testify against a man and woman for zina, but two say the woman consented and two say she was coerced. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (and Zufar), the hadd is dropped for both parties. The reason is that the act of zina is a single act involving both individuals. Consent and coercion are contradictory attributes. If the act is consensual, both are equal participants; if coerced, only the man is guilty. Since the witnesses contradict each other on this essential attribute, the subject of their testimony is inconsistent, and the required number of witnesses (four) is not fulfilled for either version. Therefore, neither can be punished. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold a different view: they argue that all four agree on the man's guilt (since both versions involve the man committing zina), so the hadd is still valid against the man. The woman is not punished because the witnesses disagree on her consent. The text then provides Abu Hanifa's stronger reasoning based on the unity of the act and the impossibility of contradictory descriptions.

Key Terms
طوع (taw'): Consent of the woman to the act of zina
نصاب الشهادة (nisab al-shahada): The required number of witnesses for a particular fact; here four witnesses agreeing on the same description
وصفان متضادان (wasfani mutadaddan): Two contradictory attributes (e.g., consent and coercion) that cannot coexist in the same act

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the hadd dropped for both the man and the woman when witnesses disagree on whether the woman consented?
A) Because consent is irrelevant in zina cases
B) Because zina is an act involving both parties, and contradictory descriptions of the same act prevent the required four-witness agreement on a single subject
C) Because the man cannot be punished without the woman also being punished
D) Because the witnesses are automatically accused of qadhf

Key Takeaway
When witnesses contradict each other on an essential attribute of the same act (like consent vs coercion), the subject of testimony differs, and the hadd is not applied to either party per Abu Hanifa's relied-upon view.

Testimony for Zina with an Absent Woman: Ruling and Distinction from Theft
Ruling on testimony for zina with absent woman, with proof and comparison to theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony for zina with an absent woman, distinguish it from theft, and explain why the mere possibility of a marriage claim does not constitute a valid shubhah.

Core Idea
In zina, testimony alone suffices for hadd even if the woman is absent; remote possibilities do not create a shubhah.

This lesson examines a key ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: the immediate establishment of hadd for zina based on testimony, even when the woman is absent. The text contrasts this with theft, where absence nullifies the claim, and then answers a subtle objection about shubhah. By the end, you will understand the Hanafi principle that speculative doubts do not block hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
The text states that if witnesses testify to a man's zina with an absent woman, he is hadded by consensus of the four imams. Note the condition: the witnesses must know the woman.
This ruling applies whether the proof is testimony or confession.
2
Locate the Dalil and Derive the Principle
The Prophet's stoning of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah, who confessed to zina with absent persons, shows that the presence of the other party is not a condition for establishing hadd.
The dalil is an ahad hadith, but its meaning is qat'i (definitive) for this ruling.
3
Distinguish from Theft
In theft, the claim of the absent owner is a condition for cutting the hand; absence loses the claim. This difference stems from the nature of the rights involved: in zina, it is a public right (haqq Allah), while theft involves a private right (haqq al-'abd).
The text explicitly says 'bi-khilaf al-sariqah'.
4
Analyse the Shubhah Objection and Hanafi Response
The objection: the man might claim marriage, creating a shubhah that should avert hadd. The Hanafi response distinguishes: a direct claim of marriage is a shubhah, but the mere possibility of such a claim is only a 'shubhah of a shubhah' and is ignored. Otherwise, all hudud would be blocked.
This distinction is crucial: only an actual claimed shubhah averts hadd, not a hypothetical one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الشهادة على الزنا والرجوع عنها
ولو أثبتوا زناه بغائبة حد، بخلاف السرقة
ولو أثبتوا زناه إلخ أي إذا شهدوا على رجل أنه زنى بفلانة وهي غائبة عن مجلس القاضي وهم يعرفونها حد الرجل بإجماع الأربعة وكذا لو أقر بالزنا بغائبة يقام عليه الحد بالإجماع؛ لأنه عليه رجم ماعزاً والغامدية حين أقرا بالزنا بغائبين. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٨٦/١] (فتح)
بخلاف السرقه: أي إذا شهدت الشهود أنه سرق من فلان الغائب لا يجب الحد فلا يقطع يده؛ لأن بالغيبة تفوت الدعوى، وهي شرط في السرقة دون الزنا، فإن قلت: ينبغي أن لا يحد في الزنا أيضاً حتى يحضر الغائب؛ لاحتمال أن يدعي النكاح فيكون شبهة قلت: دعوى النكاح شبهة؛ لاحتمال الصدق فتعتبر، واحتمال الدعوى شبهة الشبهة فلا تعتبر ؛ لأن اعتبارها يؤدي إلى سد باب الحدود ولا ينتقض هذا بالقصاص إذا كان بين شريكين، وكان أحدهما غائباً لا يتمكن الحاضر من الاستيفاء؛ لاحتمال العفو من الغائب؛ لأن العفو حقيقة المسقط، واحتماله يكون شبهة المسقط لا شبهة الشبهة. (عيني، فتح)
The chapter of testimony for zina and retracting therefrom. And if they establish his zina with an absent woman, he is hadded, unlike theft. 'And if they establish his zina etc.': meaning if witnesses testify against a man that he committed zina with a certain woman while she is absent from the judge's session and they know her, the man is hadded by the consensus of the four imams, and similarly if he confesses to zina with an absent woman, the hadd is established against him by consensus, because the Prophet ﷺ stoned Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah when they confessed to zina with absent persons. 'Unlike theft': meaning if the witnesses testify that he stole from an absent person, the hadd is not obligatory, so his hand is not cut, because by absence the claim is lost, and [the claim] is a condition in theft but not in zina. If you say: It should be that he is not hadded in zina either until the absent person is present, due to the possibility that he might claim marriage, which would be a shubhah. I say: The claim of marriage is a shubhah [itself] due to the possibility of truth, so it is considered, but the possibility of the claim is a shubhah of a shubhah, so it is not considered, because its consideration would lead to the closure of the hadd punishments. And this is not invalidated by qisas if between two partners and one of them is absent, the present one cannot exact retaliation due to the possibility of pardon from the absent one, because pardon is the actual remover, and its possibility is a shubhah of the remover, not a shubhah of a shubhah.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that testimony for zina against a man is effective even when the woman is absent from court. The proof is the practice of the Prophet ﷺ who implemented the hadd on Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah based on their confessions, even though the other party was not present. The text contrasts this with theft, where the claimant's presence is a condition for establishing the hadd (cutting the hand). The commentary addresses an objection: why not require presence in zina to avoid potential shubhah (doubt), e.g., the man might claim marriage. The response distinguishes between a direct shubhah (the claim of marriage itself) which is considered, and a shubhah of a shubhah (the mere possibility of such a claim), which is not, because giving weight to remote possibilities would undermine all hadd punishments. This reasoning is consistent with the Hanafi principle of blocking means to closure of hadd.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt that averts the hadd punishment; here, the possibility of a marriage claim that could create doubt.
إجماع الأربعة (ijma' al-arba'ah): Consensus of the four Sunni imams (Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad).
شبهة الشبهة (shubhat al-shubhah): A doubt upon a doubt; a second-level speculation that is not considered legally effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The man is not hadded until the woman is present.
B) The man is hadded because the woman's presence is not a condition for hadd in zina.
C) The man is hadded only if the woman confirms the testimony.
D) The man is not hadded because the absence creates a shubhah.

Key Takeaway
The hadd for zina is established by testimony or confession regardless of the other party's presence, and only a direct shubhah averts the punishment; speculative possibilities do not.

Confession of Zina with an Unknown Woman
Ruling on confession of zina with an unknown woman and its proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school applies hadd to a confessor who claims not to know the woman and apply the principle of remote possibilities (ihtimal ba'id).

Core Idea
A remote possibility does not create legal doubt; otherwise, hadd punishments would be unenforceable.

This lesson tackles a specific scenario: a man confesses to zina but says he does not know the woman. Should the hadd be applied? The Hanafi ruling might seem strict, but it is based on a rational principle: a man knows his own wife and slave. Allowing a remote, far-fetched possibility to create doubt would open the door to avoiding punishment entirely. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Scenario
The confession is to zina with an unknown woman. The ruling is that the confessor is subject to hadd (punishment).
The ruling is clear: he is hadded.
2
Understand the Proof
The proof is rational: a man knows his own wife and slave-girl. He cannot genuinely be ignorant of them. The remote possibility that the woman could be his wife or slave through obscure means is too far-fetched.
This is a 'dalil aqli' (rational proof).
3
Apply the Principle of Ihtimal Ba'id
Hanafi usul distinguishes between likely possibilities (ihtimal qarib) and remote possibilities (ihtimal ba'id). Only the former creates valid legal doubt (shubhah). Here, the possibility is remote and thus ignored.
The term 'ihtimal ba'id' appears in the text.
4
Consider the Consequence
If such remote possibilities were accepted, it would lead to the closure of hadd punishments (insidad bab al-hudud). This is against the maqasid (goals) of Sharia to preserve lineage and deter crime.
The text explicitly states this consequence.
5
State the Final Ruling
Therefore, the mufta bihi position is that the confessor is hadded. This is the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أقر بالزنا بمجهولة حد
حد: أي إذا أقر رجل أنه زنى بامرأة لا يعرفها حد المقر؛ لأنه لا يخفى عليه امرأته ولا أمته خصوصاً إذا لم يكن له امرأة ولا أمة ولو كانت تعرفها ولا يعتبر الاحتمال البعيد بأن كانت أمته بجهة من الجهات كالإرث، وهو لا يعرف ذلك، أو بالتوالد من مملوكاته أو من مملوكات آبائه؛ لأن اعتبار الاحتمال البعيد موهوم فلا يعتبر؛ ولأن ذلك يؤدي إلى انسداد باب الحدود
And if he confesses to zina with an unknown woman, he is hadded. Explanation: meaning if a man confesses that he committed zina with a woman he does not know, the confessor is hadded, because his wife and his slave-girl are not hidden from him, especially if he has no wife or slave-girl, even if she knows him. And the remote possibility that she could be his slave-girl through some means, such as inheritance and he does not know, or through offspring from his slaves or those of his fathers, is not considered, because consideration of a remote possibility is speculative and thus not taken into account, and because that would lead to the closure of the hadd punishments.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This ruling addresses a confessor who claims he does not know the woman with whom he committed zina. The Hanafi position is that the remote possibility that the woman might be his wife or slave-girl is not sufficient to create a shubhah that would avert the hadd. This is because it is extremely unlikely that a Muslim man would be ignorant of his own wife or slave. Considering such remote possibilities would effectively allow anyone to avoid hadd by claiming ignorance, thus leading to the closure of hadd punishments. The principle is that only likely possibilities create valid shubhah, not far-fetched ones.

Key Terms
احتمال بعيد (ihtimal ba'id): A remote possibility that is too far-fetched to create legal doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) He is not hadded due to the shubhah of the woman possibly being his wife.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not consider remote possibilities (ihtimal ba'id) as valid shubhah to avert hadd, because a man knows his own wife and slave, and ignoring this would render hadd punishments ineffective.

Effect of Witness Disagreement on Consent in Zina Hadd
Intra-Hanafi dispute on contradictory testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when witnesses differ on the woman's consent, and explain the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Contradictory testimony on consent voids the hadd for both according to Abu Hanifa.

What happens when four witnesses agree that zina occurred but disagree on whether the woman consented or was forced? This text presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa and Zufar argue the testimony is contradictory and thus invalid, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue the man's act is agreed upon, so he should be punished. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your grasp of testimony requirements in hudud.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Four witnesses testify to a specific act of zina. Two say the woman consented (taw'), two say she was forced (ikrah). Identify the key points of agreement and disagreement.
The act is the same; only the woman's state differs.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa (and Zufar): no hadd for either. Sahibayn: hadd for the man only. Note the thamarah (practical outcome).
The dispute is about consistency of testimony, not the act's occurrence.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Their reasoning: the witnesses agreed on the man's voluntary act (he chose to commit zina). The disagreement only concerns the woman; therefore, the man's hadd is established.
They treat the act as separable: the man's guilt is independent of the woman's status.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa: zina is a single act performed by both. It cannot be both consensual and forced simultaneously. The contradictory descriptions mean no single version has four witnesses, so the entire testimony fails.
He views the act as indivisible; the woman's state defines the nature of the act.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The passage does not specify which view is mufta bihi. In later Hanafi practice, the Sahibayn's view is often relied upon for hadd. Consider why consistency in testimony is central.
This shows the importance of tawatur (consistency) in testimonial evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شهدوا بذلك لا : أي إذا شهد أربعة أنه زنى بفلانة، واختلفوا في طوع المرأة، فقال اثنان منهم: بأنه زنى بها بطوعها، وقال الآخرون: بل استكرهها، سقط الحد عن الرجل والمرأة جميعاً عند أبي حنيفة، وهو قول زفر، وقالا: يحد الرجل خاصة؛ لأنهم اتفقوا في طوع الرجل، واختلفوا في طوع المرأة فلا تحد، بل يحد الرجل، ولأبي حنيفة: أن المشهود به قد اختلف؛ لأن الزنا فعل واحد يقوم بهما، وكل فعل يقوم بهما لا يتصف بوصفين متضادين، والشهود أثبتوا له وصفين متضادين؛ لأن الطوع يوجب اشتراكهما في الزنا، والكره يوجب انفراد الرجل به، واجتماع الوصفين المتضادين متعذر ، فكان كل واحد منهما خلاف الآخر، واختلف المشهود به، ولم يتم على كل واحد منهما نصاب الشهادة فلا يحد أحد. (عيني، فتح)
And if they testify to that, not [valid]? Explanation: If four testify that he committed zina with a certain woman, and they differ on the woman's consent, two saying he committed zina with her by her consent, and the others saying he forced her, the hadd is dropped from both the man and the woman according to Abu Hanifa, and this is the view of Zufar. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the man is hadded specifically, because they agreed on the man's consent (i.e., that he acted voluntarily), but differed on the woman's consent, so she is not hadded, but the man is hadded. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the thing testified to has become contradictory, because zina is a single act performed by both, and any act performed by both cannot be described by two contradictory attributes. The witnesses have attributed two contradictory attributes to it, because consent requires their mutual participation in the zina, while force requires the man alone to be the perpetrator. The combination of two contradictory attributes is impossible, so each testimony contradicts the other, and the thing testified to is disputed. Therefore, the required number of witnesses (four) is not completed for any one version, so no one is hadded.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage discusses a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school. The scenario: four witnesses testify to a specific act of zina, but they disagree on whether the woman consented or was forced. Abu Hanifa (and Zufar) rule that the hadd is dropped for both parties because the subject of testimony is inconsistent: the act cannot be both consensual and forced simultaneously. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the hadd should be applied to the man because the witnesses agreed that the man committed the act voluntarily; the only disagreement is about the woman, so she is spared but the man is not. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is based on the unity of the act: since zina is one act involving both, contradictory descriptions render the testimony insufficient to establish any single version with the requisite four witnesses. The passage does not indicate which view is preferred, but in later Hanafi practice, the opinion of the Sahibayn is often relied upon in such cases.

Key Terms
طوع (taw'): Consent of the woman to the act of zina.
كره (ikrah): Force/duress; the woman is compelled against her will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the hadd dropped for both parties when witnesses differ on the woman's consent?
A) Because the witnesses are unreliable.
B) Because the act is one and cannot have contradictory descriptions.
C) Because the man's consent is also disputed.
D) Because the woman is always exempt from hadd.

Key Takeaway
Contradictory testimony on a material element (consent) can void the hadd for all parties under Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn uphold the man's hadd by treating the act as divisible.

Witnesses Differ on Location: No Hadd on Anyone
Ruling when four witnesses differ in the city of the act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when four witnesses for zina differ on the city of the act and explain why neither the accused nor the witnesses receive hadd.

Core Idea
When witnesses differ on location, the testimony is defective; no hadd on anyone, because the form of testimony shields them from qadhf.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the law of zina testimony: four witnesses agree on the act but disagree on the city. The Hanafi school rules that neither the accused nor the witnesses are lashed. We will explore the reasoning based on istihsan and the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Four witnesses for zina agree on the act but differ on the city (e.g., two say Kufa, two say Basra).
The difference is in the location, not in the identity of the parties or the act itself.
2
Determine the ruling on the accused
The accused is not lashed because the testimony is defective—the act is considered location-specific, so disagreement shows they did not witness the same event.
3
Determine the ruling on the witnesses
The witnesses are also not lashed for qadhf (false accusation) because their statement was in the form of testimony (shahada), which drops the hadd by istihsan.
This is the Hanafi position; Zufar disagrees.
4
Analyze the proof
Hanafis rely on istihsan: the outward form of testimony is preserved to avoid labeling Muslims as slanderers without certainty.
Istihsan overrides the qiyas that would treat them as slanderers.
5
Compare with Zufar's view
Zufar applies qiyas: since the testimony is invalid, the witnesses are essentially slanderers and should be lashed. Hanafis respond that the form matters.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): Zufar would lash the witnesses; Hanafis do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الشهادة على الزنا والرجوع عنها
أو في البلد، ولو على كلّ زناً أربعة ولو اختلفوا في بيت واحدٍ حدّ الرّجل والمرأة، ولو شهدوا على زنا امرأة وهي بكر، أو الشهود فسقة، أو شهدوا على شهادة أربعة وإن شهدَ الأصول أيضاً لم يحدّ أحد، ولو كانوا عميانا، أو محدودين في قذف أو ثلاثة حدّ الشهود لا المشهود عليه، ولو جد أحدهم عبدا أو محدودا حدوا.
أو في البلد : أي لو شهد أربعة بزناه واختلفوا في بلدة زناه بأن شهد اثنان أنه زنا بها بالكوفة، واثنان أنه زنی بها بالبصرة لا يحد أحد؛ لاختلاف الشهود به؛ لأن الفعل يختلف باختلاف الأماكن، ولا يحد الشهود أيضاً عندنا، وقال زفر: يحدون؛ لأنهم قذفة، قلنا: إن كلامهم وقع شهادة صورة، فأسقط الحد عنهم.
Book of Hudud. Chapter of Testimony for Zina and Retraction Therefrom. Or in the city, even if there are four for each zina, and if they differ in one house, the man and woman are hadd, and if they testify to zina of a woman while she is a virgin, or the witnesses are sinful, or they testify to the testimony of four even if the original witnesses also testify, no one is hadd, and if they are blind, or those who have been lashed for qadhf, or three, the witnesses are hadd, not the accused, and if a slave or one who has been lashed is found among them, they are hadd. 'Or in the city' means: if four testify to his zina and they differ in the city of his zina, such that two testify that he committed zina with her in Kufa and two that he committed zina with her in Basra, then no one is lashed due to the difference in the witnesses' testimony because the act differs according to places, and the witnesses are also not lashed according to us. Zufar said: They are lashed because they are slanderers (qadhifa). We say: Their speech occurred in the form of testimony, so the hadd is dropped from them.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on testimony for zina and retraction. The matn lists several scenarios where the hadd of zina is not applied. The first scenario is when four witnesses agree on the act but differ on the location (city). The commentator explains that such disagreement prevents the hadd from being imposed on the accused because the act of zina is considered location-specific; differing on location implies they did not all witness the same act. Furthermore, the witnesses themselves are not punished for qadhf (false accusation) because their statements technically constitute a valid testimony (shahada) in form, even though it is defective. Zufar differs, holding that the witnesses should be lashed for qadhf since they have not proven the zina. The Hanafi position is that since they claimed to be testifying, the hadd for qadhf is dropped, based on istihsan (juristic preference) that preserves the form of shahada.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina, which incurs a hadd of 80 lashes.
شهادة (shahada): Legal testimony, especially in hudud cases, typically requiring four witnesses for zina.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of abandoning a clear qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when four witnesses for zina differ on the city where the act occurred?
A) The accused is lashed and the witnesses are also lashed for qadhf.
B) The accused is lashed but the witnesses are not punished.
C) Neither the accused nor the witnesses are lashed.
D) The witnesses are lashed for qadhf but the accused is freed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, witnesses for zina who differ on the location of the act avoid hadd for qadhf because their speech took the form of testimony, and the accused also avoids hadd due to the defect.

Testimony upon Testimony in Zina Cases
Ruling on shahada 'ala shahada in zina cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shahada 'ala shahada in hudud cases and explain why it is not accepted, using the principle of dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat.

Core Idea
Shahada 'ala shahada is a substitute; hudud require certainty, so doubt blocks the hadd.

In this passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the author addresses a specific scenario in zina cases: four witnesses testify based on the testimony of another four, and even if the original witnesses also appear, no one is flogged. This ruling highlights a key Hanafi principle: hudud punishments are averted by doubts. Let's explore the legal reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand the three scenarios: (1) four branch witnesses testify on original four, (2) same but original witnesses also appear, (3) the general rule that no hadd is applied. Note the common factor: shahada 'ala shahada.
2
Classify the Testimony
Recognize that shahada 'ala shahada is a badal (substitute) for direct testimony. In Hanafi law, substitutes are only allowed when necessary (e.g., absence of original witness), not in hudud because hudud require the highest certainty.
Financial cases accept it due to necessity.
3
Apply Dar' al-Hudud bi al-Shubuhat
The possibility of falsehood in both layers of testimony creates shubha (doubt). According to the maxim 'avert hudud by doubts', the hadd is not applied. The branches are not flogged because they are merely narrating, not accusing.
Even if originals testify later, their testimony is rejected due to prior rejection.
4
State the Practical Outcome
No one is flogged in any of the three scenarios. The ruling preserves the principle that hudud are severe and require certainty; any doubt blocks them.
The four witnesses are not considered slanderers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود باب الشهادة على الزنا والرجوع عنها
أو شهدوا على شهادة أربعة وإن شهد الأصول أيضاً لم يحد أحد.
أو شهدوا على شهادة أربعة إلخ هذ مسألة ثالثة أي إذا شهد أربعة رجال وهم الفروع على شهادة أربعة الأصول بالزنا لم يحد أحد أيضاً؛ لاحتمال الكذب في شهادة الأصول وفي شهادة الفروع؛ ولأن الشهادة على الشهادة بدل والإبدال تنصب للحاجة ولا حاجة في الحدود إلى البدل ولا حد على الفروع؛ لأنهم ما نسبوا المشهود عليه إلى الزنا، وإنما حكوا شهادة الأصول، والحاكي للقذف لا يكون قاذفاً. قوله : وإن شهد الأصول أيضاً، واصل بما قبله أي وإن جاء الأصول وشهدوا على معاينة ذلك الزنا بعينه لم تقبل شهادتهم؛ لأن شهادتهم قد ردت من وجه برد شهادة الفروع في عين تلك الحادثة، ولم يحدوا أيضاً لتكامل النصاب وكون الكلام شهادة، وقوله : " لم يحد أحد أما في الصورة الأولى فلأن الزنا لا يتحقق . البكارة، وأما في الثانية لتهمة الكذب لكون الشهود فسقة، وأما في الثالثة فلما فيه من زيادة الشبهة؛ لأن احتمال الكذب فيها في موضعين في شهادة الأصول والفروع رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۸/١] (فتح)
Book of Hudud, Chapter on Testimony about Zina and Retraction thereof. Or if they testify upon the testimony of four, even if the original witnesses also testify, no one is flogged. 'Or if they testify upon the testimony of four' etc.: This is a third issue, meaning when four men, who are branches, testify upon the testimony of four original witnesses concerning zina, no one is flogged either, due to the possibility of falsehood in both the original and branch testimonies, and because testimony upon testimony is a substitute, and substitutes are established for necessity, and there is no need for substitutes in hudud. There is no hadd on the branches because they did not attribute zina to the accused, they merely narrated the original witnesses' testimony, and the narrator of slander is not a slanderer. His statement: 'Even if the original witnesses also testify,' is connected to the preceding—meaning that if the original witnesses come and testify to having witnessed that actual zina, their testimony is not accepted because their testimony was already rejected in respect of the branches' testimony in that same incident. They are also not flogged due to the completion of the required number and because the speech is testimony. His statement: 'No one is flogged'—in the first scenario because zina is not confirmed; in the second scenario due to the suspicion of falsehood because the witnesses are corrupt; in the third scenario because of increased doubt, as possibility of falsehood exists in two places: the original and branch testimonies. (Reference: Rumor al-Haqa'iq 1:388) (Fath)
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is a ruling with proof on testimony upon testimony (shahada 'ala shahada) in zina cases. The author states that if four witnesses testify based on the testimony of another four (the original witnesses), and even if the original witnesses also testify directly, no one is flogged. The commentator explains that this is because of the possibility of false testimony in both layers, and because testimony upon testimony is a substitute, and substitutes are only allowed when necessary—but there is no necessity in hudud, which are dropped by doubts. The branches are not flogged because they are merely narrating, not directly accusing. Even if the original witnesses come forward, their testimony is rejected due to the previous rejection of the branches' version, but no one is flogged because the required number of witnesses (four) is complete and the statement is considered testimony. The three scenarios described are: (1) zina not confirmed (e.g., due to virginity), (2) suspicion of falsehood due to corrupt witnesses, (3) increased doubt due to double layers of testimony. This reflects the Hanafi principle of dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat.

Key Terms
الشهادة على الشهادة (al-shahada 'ala al-shahada): Testimony upon testimony: when a witness testifies to having heard another witness's testimony, rather than firsthand knowledge.
بدل (badal): Substitute: shahada 'ala shahada is a substitute for direct testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the original witnesses must be present in person.
B) Because testimony upon testimony is not accepted in hudud due to the principle of avoiding hadd through doubts.
C) Because the branches are considered slanderers and are flogged instead.
D) Because the accused must confess first.

Key Takeaway
Shahada 'ala shahada does not lead to hadd punishment because it creates doubt, and hudud are averted by doubts.

Witness Impediments in Hadd Cases: Blind, Hadd-offender, and Insufficient Number
Ruling when witnesses are blind, hadd-punished, or less than four

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios in which witnesses of zina are flogged instead of the accused, and explain the Hanafi rationale for each.

Core Idea
Witnesses in hadd cases must meet strict standards; defect in vision, past conviction, or insufficient number makes them liable to flogging.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines when the witnesses themselves become the punished party. The Hanafi school holds that in certain situations, the accused is spared and the witnesses face the hadd of qadhf. Understanding these rules is crucial for the proper administration of justice and for distinguishing the Hanafi approach from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
From the text, list the three conditions of the witnesses that lead to the witnesses being flogged: (1) blind, (2) previously flogged for qadhf, (3) less than four men.
The text also mentions a fourth scenario: after the accused has been flogged, a witness is found to be a slave or previously flogged; then the witnesses are flogged.
2
Explain the rationale for each
For blind witnesses: their testimony cannot establish property (which can be established by doubt), so how can it establish hadd, which is averted by doubts? For those flogged for qadhf: their testimony is permanently rejected. For three witnesses: they fall short of the required four, thus they become slanderers (qadifun).
The principle is that hadd is averted by doubts (shubhat), and defective testimony creates such doubts.
3
Identify the Quranic evidence
The ruling is based on Quran 24:4, which commands flogging the accuser when four witnesses are not produced. The imperative 'fajliduhum' indicates obligation.
The verse is the foundation for the hadd of qadhf.
4
Compare with Shafi'i position
The text notes that al-Shafi'i considers a fasiq (sinful person) unfit for testimony, like a slave. The Hanafi position is more specific: only certain defects (blindness, prior hadd, insufficiency) disqualify in hudud.
This difference stems from varying interpretations of what constitutes 'adalah (uprightness).
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider: Two men and two women testify against someone for zina. Are the witnesses flogged? In Hanafi law, women are not acceptable in zina cases, so the witnesses are considered less than four men and are flogged.
This shows the strictness of the requirement for four male witnesses in zina.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانوا عميانا، أو محدودين في قذف أو ثلاثة حدّ الشهود لا المشهود عليه، ولو أي الشهود أي أو كانوا أي في حد القذف أي أو كانوا ثلاثة النفس فجد فوجد أحدهم عبدا أو محدودا حدوا أي رجل بشهاده أربعة أي أحد الشهود الأربعة رجال وهم فلا يسقط بالاحتمال بخلاف حد القذف؛ لأنه لا يجب بالقذف، بل بالعجز عن إقامة البينة فيه خلاف الشافعي بناء على أن الفاسق عنده ليس من أهل الشهادة فهو كالعبد عنده. (عيني، مستخلص)
And if they [the witnesses] were blind, or had been flogged in qadhf, or were three [men], then the witnesses are flogged, not the accused. (Commentary: 'and if they were blind' etc.) This is a condition that includes three images: the consequence is that the witnesses are flogged, not the accused—meaning if the witnesses in zina were blind or had been flogged (i.e., punished) in qadhf, or were three men, then in all these images the witnesses are flogged, because the testimony of the blind and those flogged does not establish property (which can be established by doubt), so how can it establish hadd, which is averted by doubts? As for the testimony of three, it is slander (qadhf) due to lacking the required number, so they are flogged, and the accused is not flogged because zina is not proven. (Rumor al-Haqa'iq 1:388) (Fath). And if one of them is found to be a slave or one flogged in qadhf, then they are flogged because they are slanderers. In this there is a difference with al-Shafi'i, based on his view that a sinful person is not fit for testimony, being like a slave in his view. ('Ayni, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents three scenarios where the witnesses themselves are flogged instead of the accused: (1) if the witnesses are blind; (2) if they have previously been flogged for false accusation (qadhf); (3) if they are less than four men (three or fewer). The Hanafi reasoning is that the testimony of blind or previously flogged witnesses is not reliable enough to establish hadd, which is averted by doubts. Three witnesses also fall short of the required four, making them qadifun (slanderers) subject to the hadd of qadhf. The text also mentions a fourth scenario (later in the same passage) where after the accused has been flogged, it is discovered that one witness was a slave or had been flogged for qadhf; then the witnesses are flogged. The commentator notes a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers a sinful person (fasiq) unfit for testimony, analogous to a slave. This intra-madhhab point is clarified: for the Hanafis, the disqualification here is specific to the conditions mentioned, not general sinfulness.

Key Terms
محدود في قذف (mahdud fi qadhf): A person who has been flogged for false accusation (qadhf), thus his testimony is rejected in the future.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are each flogged for qadhf.
B) They are each given a reprimand.
C) The accused is flogged for zina.
D) The witnesses are imprisoned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, defective witness conditions in zina cases do not exonerate the witnesses; they become the accused and face the hadd of qadhf.

Deficient Witnesses After Hadd Execution: Testimony Becomes Qadhf
Ruling on defective witnesses after hadd execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a witness is found to be a slave or mahdud after hadd execution and explain the role of hasab in transforming testimony into qadhf.

Core Idea
Testimony with deficient witnesses reverts to qadhf, making the accusers liable for flogging.

In hudud cases, the requirement of four upright witnesses is strict. When after the hadd has been executed it is discovered that one witness lacked capacity or had been previously flogged for qadhf, the testimony becomes invalid and the accusers become liable for flogging. This lesson examines the legal basis and the concept of hasab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect in witnesses
Determine what disqualifies a witness: being a slave (lack of freedom) or having been flogged for qadhf (lack of probity). These defects reduce the number of valid witnesses.
Note that blindness or disbelief are also disqualifications mentioned in glosses.
2
Apply the Quranic verse
Refer to Surah al-Nur 24:4, which prescribes flogging for those who accuse without four witnesses. Here the number is effectively three or less, so the verse applies.
3
Understand hasab
Hasab is the probity that distinguishes testimony from slander. A witness has two hasab: concealment and testimony. When the number is incomplete, both are absent, so the act is qadhf.
The concept is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Derive the ruling
Since hasab is absent, the witnesses are flogged for qadhf. The executed person is considered innocent and blood money may be due if killed.
The second part of the passage discusses arsh, which is covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو محدوداً [ أي الشهود كلهم؛ لأنهم قذفة. (عيني)] في قذف أو وجد أعمى أو كافرا. (ط) حدوا: أي إذا شهد أربعة بالزنا، فضرب المشهود عليه بشهادتهم، ثم وجد أحدهم عبداً أو محدوداً في القذف، فإنهم يحدون؛ لأنهم قذفة؛ إذ الشهود ثلاثة؛ إذ لا حسبة عند نقصان العدد، فإن الشاهد مخير بين حسبتين كما مر، وههنا لم يوجد منه حسبة الستر وهو ظاهر، ولا حسبة أداء الشهادة أيضاً؛ لنقصان عددهم؛ لقوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ يَرْمُونَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ ثُمَّ لَمْ يَأْتُوا بِأَرْبَعَةِ شُهَدَاءَ فَاجْلِدُوهُمْ ثَمَانِينَ جَلْدَةً ﴾ (النور:٤)، وإذا لم توجد الحسبة ثبت القذف؛ لأن خروج الشهادة عن القذف إنما يكون باعتبار الحسبة. (مستخلص، فتح)
وأرش ضربه ،هدر، وإن رجم فديته على بيت المال، ولو رجع الرجم أي دية المرحوم حد وغرم ربع الدية، وقبله حدوا الراجع وحده ضمن الراجع اتفاقاً أحد الأربعة بعد من الشهود
وأرش ضربه هدر [ أي باطل لا شيء فيه على أحد. (معدن)] أرش الضرب هو أجرة الطبيب وثمن الأدوية، والمراد ههنا دية الجراحة أي إذا ضرب الجلاد المشهود عليه بأمر القاضي وجرحه السياط فديته لا يجب على أحد عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الجرح إما من خرق الجلاد بأن لا يحسن الضرب أو من رقة بشرة المضروب، فلا يضمنه أحد، وقالا: يجب على بيت المال؛ لأن الجرح موجب لتلف النفس، وقد ظهر خطأ الإمام فيجب في بيت المال كما في الرجم، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ومعرفة الأرش أن يقوم المجلود عبداً سليماً عن الجراحة ويقوّم وبه هذا الأثر، وينظر ما نقص به القيمة فينقص من الدية مثله، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو مات من الضرب تجب الدية في بيت المال عندهما خلافاً له، وكذا لو رجع الشهود وقد جرحته السياط أو مات من الضرب لا يضمنون عنده خلافاً لهما. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۸/١]
Or one flogged in qadhf, or if a blind or disbeliever is found. (T) They are flogged: i.e., if four testify to zina, and the accused is flogged by their testimony, then it is discovered that one of them was a slave or had been flogged in qadhf, then they are flogged because they are slanderers—since the witnesses are [effectively] three, as there is no probity (hasab) when the number is deficient. The witness is between two choices as mentioned, and here there is no probity of concealment, which is apparent, nor probity of delivering testimony due to deficiency in number, because Allah says: 'And those who accuse chaste women then do not bring four witnesses, flog them eighty lashes.' When probity is absent, qadhf is established, because testimony ceases to be qadhf only by virtue of probity. (Mustakhlas, Fath) And the compensation for his beating is void. If he is stoned, his diyah is on the treasury. And if one of the four retracts after stoning, the retractor is flogged and pays a quarter of the diyah. And before [stoning], they are all flogged. The retractor alone is liable by agreement. And the compensation for his beating is void, meaning null, nothing is due from anyone. (Ma'din) The compensation for beating is the doctor's fee and cost of medicine; here it means the diyah for injury. That is, if the flogger strikes the accused by judge's order and wounds him with the whip, his diyah is not due from anyone according to Abu Hanifa, because the wound is either due to the flogger's poor skill or the softness of the victim's skin, so no one guarantees it. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: it is due on the treasury because the wound leads to loss of life, and the imam's error has become apparent, so it should be on the treasury as in the case of stoning. The three (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad?) agreed with them. The method of determining arsh: value the flogged person as a sound slave without injury, then value him with this wound, and see the decrease in value; then deduct that proportion from the diyah. On this basis, if he dies from the beating, the diyah is on the treasury according to them, contrary to him. Similarly, if the witnesses retract after his wounds or death from beating, they do not guarantee according to him, contrary to them. (Reference: Rumor al-Haqa'iq 1:388)
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage combines two related rulings: (1) When after the accused has been flogged, it is discovered that one of the four witnesses was a slave or had been punished for qadhf, then all the witnesses are flogged for qadhf because the effective number of valid witnesses falls to three or less. The Quranic verse (24:4) is invoked: lacking four witnesses makes the accusers liable for flogging. The basis is 'hasab' (probity/deterrent effect) which is absent. (2) The compensation (arsh) for injuries caused by the flogging is a matter of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa holds it is void (no one liable), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold it is on the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The reasoning is over causation: Abu Hanifa sees the wound as due to the flogger's ineptitude or the victim's soft skin, not the imam's order; the two companions see the imam's order as the cause of the error, and since the imam acts for the Muslims, the treasury bears the loss. The same ikhtilaf applies if the victim dies from the flogging. If the witnesses retract after injury, Abu Hanifa holds them not liable, while the two companions hold them liable. This showcases Hanafi intra-school reasoning on causation and public liability.

Key Terms
حسبة (hasab): Probity or deterrent effect that distinguishes testimony from slander; it is the quality that makes testimony valid and prevents the witness from being a slanderer.
أرش الضرب (arsh al-darb): Compensation for injuries caused by flogging, including medical expenses and diyah for wounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on four witnesses to zina if one is later found to be a slave after execution?
A) The slave is flogged; the others are free.
B) All four are flogged for qadhf.
C) Only the three free witnesses are flogged.
D) None are flogged because execution already occurred.

Key Takeaway
When the number of valid witnesses falls below four, the testimony becomes qadhf, and the accusers are flogged.

Compensation for Flogging Injuries: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Dispute over arsh for flogging injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding liability for injuries caused by flogging during hadd execution and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa voids arsh for flogging injuries; the two companions place it on the treasury, a view later adopted as the official position.

The execution of hadd punishment can sometimes cause injury or death. Who bears the financial liability? This lesson examines the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, along with the reasoning behind each view, and how the Hanafi school finally settled the issue.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A person is flogged by order of the imam for a hadd (like zina) based on testimony. The flogging causes injury (wounds) or death.
2
Explain Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa holds that the arsh (compensation for wounds) is void. No one is liable because the wound is due to the flogger's poor skill or the victim's soft skin, not the imam's order.
He distinguishes this from stoning where the imam's order directly causes death.
3
Explain the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the arsh/diyah is on the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The imam's order caused the injury, and since the imam acts for the community, the treasury bears the loss.
They analogize to the case of stoning (رجم).
4
Extend to death and retraction
The same ikhtilaf applies if the victim dies from the flogging. Also, if the witnesses retract after injury or death, Abu Hanifa holds them not liable, while the Sahibayn hold them liable.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa follows the Sahibayn, consistent with the three other schools. This is the operative position today.
The tarjih is based on majority practice and justice.

Retraction of a Witness After Stoning: Flogging and Diyah
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a witness who retracts after stoning, explain the legal basis for the hadd and diyah, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i and Zufar positions.

Core Idea
A retracting witness in a hadd case is liable for qadhf and proportional diyah — his testimony effectively becomes slander.

This passage addresses a critical scenario in criminal testimony: what happens when a witness in a stoning case retracts after the accused has been executed? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer that combines criminal (hadd) and civil (diyah) liability, reflecting the seriousness of false testimony while avoiding the extreme of qisas. Understanding this ruling requires tracing the logic of proportional responsibility and the retroactive transformation of testimony into slander.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering event and the Hanafi ruling
Recognize that the passage concerns one of four witnesses retracting after the accused has been stoned. The Hanafi ruling: the retractor is flogged (hadd for qadhf) and pays one-quarter of the diyah.
This ruling is for retraction after execution; retraction before execution results in all witnesses being flogged.
2
Trace the legal reasoning for the hadd
The retraction transforms the original testimony into qadhf (false accusation of zina). Since the accused is now dead, the slander is against a deceased person, which still incurs the hadd in Hanafi law.
Zufar disputes this, arguing death of the accused removes the hadd, but the majority hold the hadd remains.
3
Understand the financial liability (diyah)
The four witnesses together constitute the full proof. Retraction by one leaves three witnesses, which is three-quarters of the proof. Therefore, the retractor is responsible for one-quarter of the death, hence 1/4 diyah.
The principle: liability is proportional to the share of testimony.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Zufar)
Zufar argues no hadd because the accused is dead, and slandering a dead person might be doubtful given the judge's order. The Hanafi response: the death does not negate the hadd; the judgment is dissolved for the retractor, so he faces the hadd.
The majority view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is the mufta bihi.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the retractor should be executed for causing a Muslim's death. The Hanafi response: the witness did not directly kill; the judge executed based on testimony. Retraction shows slander, not murder, so the appropriate punishment is hadd for qadhf, not qisas.
The practical difference: Shafi'i execution vs. Hanafi flogging + diyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رجع الرجم أي دية المرحوم حد وغرم ربع الدية، وقبله حدوا الراجع وحده ضمن الراجع اتفاقاً أحد الأربعة بعد من الشهود
ولو رجع أحد الأربعة إلخ أي إذا شهد أربعة على رجل محصن بالزنا، فرجم ثم رجع أحدهم حد الراجع وحده الدية، أما الغرامة، فلأن الباقي ثلاثة أرباع نصاب الشهادة، والاعتبار للباقي، فيكون التلف بشهادة الراجع ربع الحق، وقال الشافعي: يجب قتله لكونه سبباً لقتل المسلم، وأما الحد فمذهب علمائنا الثلاثة؛ لانقلاب شهادته بالرجوع قذفاً، وقال زفر: لا يحد؛ لأنه إن كان قاذف . فقد سقط بالموت، وإن كان قاذف ميت فهو مرجوم بحكم القاضي، وذلك يورث الشبهة، قلنا: إنه قاذف ميت إلا أن شهادته بالرجوع انقلبت قذفاً، قاذفا. فصار بعد الموت، ومن قذف ميتاً يلزم الحد، ولم يبق مرجوماً بحكم القاضي؛ لانفساخ الحكم بانفساخ الحجة في حقه؛ لأن زعمه معتبر في حقه، وكلما رجع واحد حد وغرم ربع الدية؛ لأن تلف النفس بشهادتهم فيضمنون. (مستخلص، فتح)
And if one of the four retracts after stoning: the retractor is flogged and pays a quarter of the diyah. Before [stoning], they are all flogged. The retractor alone is liable by agreement. (The passage continues with commentary): 'And if one of the four retracts' etc.: That is, if four testify against a married man for zina, and he is stoned, then one retracts, the retractor alone is flogged and pays the diyah. As for the guarantee, because the remaining [witnesses] are three-quarters of the full number, and consideration is given to the remainder, so the death is attributed to the retractor's testimony as one quarter of the right. Al-Shafi'i said: He should be executed because he was a cause of killing a Muslim. As for the hadd, it is the view of our three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) because his testimony by retraction becomes qadhf. Zufar said: He should not be flogged because if he is a slanderer, the hadd has lapsed by the death [of the accused], and if he slanders a dead person, he is stoned by the judge's order, which creates doubt. We say: He is a slanderer of a dead person, but his testimony by retraction turned into qadhf, so he becomes a slanderer after death. And whoever slanders a dead person incurs the hadd, and he is no longer considered stoned by the judge's order because the judgment is dissolved when the evidence is dissolved in his case, since his claim is considered in his own case. Whenever one retracts, he is flogged and pays a quarter of the diyah, because the loss of life is through their testimony, so they guarantee it. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage deals with the retraction of one of four witnesses after the accused has been stoned to death. The Hanafi ruling is that the retracting witness is flogged for qadhf (80 lashes) and pays one-quarter of the diyah (blood money) for the deceased. The reasoning: the remaining three witnesses represent three-quarters of the complete testimony, so the retractor's portion is one-quarter, and thus he guarantees that proportion. The hadd of qadhf applies because his retraction transforms his testimony into slander. Imam al-Shafi'i differs, arguing that the retractor should be executed for causing a Muslim's death. Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl (a Hanafi) also differs, arguing that no hadd is due because the accused is dead and slandering a dead person might be mitigated by doubt. The Hanafi response is that death does not eliminate the hadd, and the previous judgment dissolves in relation to the retractor, so he faces the hadd. This highlights a key principle: the witnesses' retraction retroactively shows their testimony was false, making them liable for both criminal (hadd) and civil (diyah) consequences.

Key Terms
الرجوع عن الشهادة (al-ruju' 'an al-shahada): Retraction of testimony after judgment has been executed; it converts the witness into a slanderer (qadif).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if one of four witnesses retracts his testimony after the accused has been stoned to death, what is his punishment?
A) He is executed for causing a Muslim's death.
B) He is imprisoned for perjury.
C) He is flogged for qadhf and pays one-quarter of the diyah.
D) He is only flogged for qadhf, no financial payment.

Key Takeaway
A retracting witness after stoning is liable for hadd al-qadhf and proportional diyah based on his share of the testimony.

Retraction of a Witness Before Stoning: The Hanafi Dispute
Ruling when a witness retracts before stoning execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf versus Muhammad al-Shaybani on the effect of a witness's retraction before execution of hadd, and explain the principle of al-imda' fi al-hudud mulhaq.

Core Idea
A single retraction before stoning collapses the entire testimony in the view of the two shaykhs, but only affects the retractor according to Muhammad.

When four witnesses testify to zina, the judge orders stoning. But if one retracts before the execution, what happens? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad al-Shaybani, with al-Shafi'i aligning with Muhammad. At stake is whether the testimony and its execution form a single legal process or are independent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and default ruling
Understand the context: four witnesses testify, judgment is passed, but before stoning one retracts. The default ruling stated first is that all four witnesses are flogged for qadhf.
The text says 'وقبله حدوا' (and before it they are all flogged) as the initial statement.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
The two shaykhs hold that all four witnesses are flogged because the execution (stoning) is annexed to the testimony; any defect in testimony before execution nullifies the entire case, making all liable for false accusation.
This is based on the principle: 'al-imda' fi al-hudud mulhaq'.
3
State the position of Muhammad al-Shaybani
Muhammad holds that only the retracting witness is flogged. The judgment for the others remains valid because the testimony was complete at the time of judgment; retraction only affects the retractor's own testimony.
Al-Shafi'i and Zufar agree with Muhammad.
4
Identify the key principle: al-imda' fi al-hudud mulhaq
The two shaykhs rely on the principle that execution in hudud is annexed to the testimony, treating the entire process as one. Muhammad sees testimony and execution as separable.
This principle is crucial: it determines whether a defect before execution taints the whole.
5
Compare the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: all four flogged vs. only one flogged. The accused is not stoned in either view because the proof is no longer complete.
This shows how a difference in legal theory leads to different outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبله حدوا أي لو رجع أحد من الشهود قبل الرجم بعد القضاء حدوا كلهم ولا رجم على المشهود عليه لعدم ثبوت الزنا، وقال محمد الراجع وحده إن رجع بعد القضاء، وبه قال الشافعي وزفر؛ لأن القضاء حصل بالشهادة، فرجوعه يبطل شهادته في حقه لا في حق غيره، وللشيخين: أن الإمضاء أي استيفاء الرجم في باب الحدود ملحق
And before it (i.e., before stoning), they are all flogged: i.e., if one of the witnesses retracts before stoning after the judgment, they are all flogged, and there is no stoning on the accused due to lack of proof of zina. Muhammad said: only the retractor [is flogged] if he retracts after the judgment, and al-Shafi'i and Zufar agreed with him, because the judgment was established by the testimony, so his retraction nullifies his testimony concerning himself but not concerning others. For the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): that the execution (i.e., carrying out stoning) in hudud is annexed [to the original testimony].
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of retraction by one of four witnesses after the judge has ruled the stoning but before it is carried out. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), all four witnesses are flogged for qadhf because the entire testimony collapses due to the defect, and since the execution is annexed to the testimony, the retraction of one invalidates the whole case. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that only the retracting witness is flogged, because the judgment was based on all four, and one retraction does not undo the others' roles. The accused is not stoned in either view because the proof is no longer complete. Al-Shafi'i and Zufar agree with Muhammad on the limited effect. The commentary mentions that the reasoning of the two shaykhs is that execution in hudud is 'mulhaq' (annexed), meaning the act of stoning is considered a continuation of the testimony, so any defect in the testimony affects the execution. This shows a difference in how they understand the relationship between testimony and execution: the shaykhs see it as a single process, while Muhammad sees the testimony as independent from the execution.

Key Terms
الإمضاء في الحدود ملحق (al-imda' fi al-hudud mulhaq): The execution (carrying out hadd) is annexed to the testimony, meaning it is considered part of the same legal process.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if one of the four witnesses in a zina case retracts after the judge has ordered stoning but before it is carried out, what happens?
A) Only the retracting witness is flogged for qadhf.
B) All four witnesses are flogged for qadhf.
C) The retracting witness is executed.
D) The accused is stoned anyway and the witness pays diyah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh (zahir al-riwayah), the retraction of a witness before execution of hadd nullifies the entire testimony, leading to all witnesses being flogged for false accusation.
