Specific Utterances Constituting Qadhf: Anger, Metaphor, and Madhhab Differences
Rulings on whether certain statements incur hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on which utterances incur hadd for qadhf, apply the condition of anger, and distinguish the Hanafi view from other schools regarding metaphorical statements.

Core Idea
Hadd for qadhf requires a clear accusation of zina; metaphorical insults in anger do not trigger the hadd penalty.

This lesson explores the rulings on qadhf (false accusation of zina) in Hanafi fiqh, particularly focusing on which specific statements incur the hadd penalty. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the rule that denying someone's paternity in anger triggers hadd, but the same statement in a non-angry context is only reproach. The commentary expands with examples of statements that do not constitute qadhf, such as calling an Arab 'Nabati' or attributing someone to an uncle, and highlights a key inter-madhhab dispute with Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its conditions
The matn states that if someone says 'You are not your father's son' in anger, hadd applies, provided the mother is free and Muslim. In non-anger, it is considered reproach and no hadd.
The anger condition is decisive: without it, the statement is ambiguous and not qadhf.
2
Understand the proof (dalil) through istihsan
The qiyas would be that no hadd applies due to probability, but istihsan based on Ibn Mas'ud's report imposes hadd only for qadhf of a muhsana or denial from the father.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here because of the stronger public interest in preserving lineage.
3
Distinguish literal from metaphorical statements
Statements like 'You are not your father's son' in anger are literal; in non-anger they are figurative (reproach). Similarly, calling someone 'son of rainwater' or attributing to an uncle is metaphorical, not qadhf.
Customary usage determines whether an utterance is a clear accusation of zina.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute on 'Ya Nabati'
Hanafi school: calling an Arab 'Nabati' is not qadhf because it is not a clear accusation of zina; it may be an insult but not hadd. Malik, Shafi'i (one opinion), and Ahmad: hadd if intention to insult is proven.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi no hadd; others impose hadd if intent is shown.
5
Apply to slander of a deceased person
If a deceased person is slandered and they were muhsan, hadd applies and the right to demand it goes to heirs (father, son, etc.), even if the heirs are not muhsan themselves.
There is ikhtilaf: Muhammad does not allow demand by a grandson via daughter; Zufar restricts it further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قال لغيره : لست لأبيك أو لست بابن فلان في غضب حد، وفي غيره لا أي في حالة غضب ومشاجرة. في غضب : أي من قال لغيره: لست لأبيك أو لست بابن فلان لأبيه الذي يعرف له، فإن كان هذا القول في حالة الغضب حد القائل إذا كانت أمه حرة مسلمة؛ لأنه إذا كان من غير أبيه المنسوب إليه كان من الزنا ضرورة، واشترط حالة الغضب؛ لأنه في غير حالة الغضب قد يراد به المعاتبة أي أنت لا تشبه أباك في المروءة والسخاء، فلا يحد مع الاحتمال، وفي حالة الغضب يراد به الحقيقة وكذا إذا قال: إنك ابن فلان لغير أبيه يحد إذا كان في حالة المشاتمة، وفي حالة الرضا لا يحد، والقياس أن لا يكون قذفا في الأحوال كلها للاحتمال وجه الاستحسان ما روي عن ابن مسعود له قال: لا حد إلا في قذف محصنة أو نفي رجل من أبيه، ولو نفاه عن أمه بأن قال: لست لأمك لا يجب عليه الحد؛ لأنه صدق؛ لأن النسب إلى الآباء فقط. (فتح، مستخلص)
لا: أي في حالة غير غضب لا يحد. (عيني)
كنفيه عن جده، وقوله لعربي : يا نبطي ويا ابن ماء السماء ونسبته إلى عمه أو خاله أو رابه، ولو قال : يا ابن الزانية، وأمّه . . .
كنفيه عن جده: [ كما لا يحد في نفي القائل لغيره. (عيني)] مثال لعدم الحد أي كما لا يحد في نفي القاذف المقذوف عن جده بأن قال: "لست" بابن فلان وفلان جده لا يحد؛ لأنه صادق في كلامه، لأن المقذوف ابن أبيه لا ابن جده، وكذا لو نسبه إلى جده لا يحد أيضاً؛ لأنه ينسب إليه مجازاً، قال الله تعالى: ﴿كَمَا أَخْرَجَ أَبَوَيْكُمْ مِنَ الْجَنَّةِ ﴾ (الأعراف:٢٧) (مستخلص، عيني) وقوله لعربي إلخ: بالجر عطف على قوله : "كنفيه" أي وكما لا يحد في قوله لعربي، والعرب هم الجيل المعروف، وكل من سكن بلاد العرب وجزيرتها ونطق بلسان أهلها فهو عرب أي من قال لعرب: "يانبطي" بفتح الموحدة نسبة إلى النبط، وهم جيل من الناس بسواد العراق ممن يذم أحد بالنسبة إليهم لا يحد، وعند مالك والشافعي في قول، وأحمد في رواية يحد إذا نوى به الشتم وكذا لا يحد في نسبته إلى غير قبيلة، وعن مالك يحد في كل نسبة لحقه العار عربياً أو عجمياً، وقوله: "يا ابن ماء السماء" أي وكما لا يحد بقوله لغيره: يا ابن ماء السماء؛ لأنه يراد به التشبيه في الجود والسماحة والصفاء، وكان عامر بن حارثة يلقب بماء السماء لكرمه (رمز الحقائق: ٣٩٤/١) وقالا : إنه كان يقيم ماله في القحط مقام الفطر، وسميت أم المنذر بماء السماء لحسنها وجمالها، وقيل: لأولادها ماء السماء وهم ملوك العراق، فإن قيل: حالة الغضب تأبى عن هذا القصد، قلنا: نجعله سباً لنفي الشجاعة في الغضب، أما كونه نفياً موجباً للحد فلا؛ إذ لم يعهد استعماله لذلك القصد. (فتح) و نسبته إلى عمه مثال آخر لعدم الحد أي لا يحد في نسبة القاذف إلى عمه أو خاله أو مربيه؛ لأنه ينسب إليهم عادة مجازاً، وكذا إذا نسبه إلى جده، قال تعالى حكاية عن إسرائيل وبنيه: قَالُوا نَعْبُدُ إِلَهَكَ وَإِلَهَ آبَائِكَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَإِسْمَاعِيلَ وَإِسْحَاقَ (البقرة:١٣٣) وإبراهيم كان جده، وإسحاق أباه وإسماعيل عمه وقال عليه: "الخال أب"، وقال تعالى: حكاية عن نوح عليه : وإنَّ ابْنِي مِنْ أَهْلِي (هود: ٤٥) قيل: إنه كان ابن امرأته، وقال أشهب: يحد في نسبته إلى هؤلاء عند المشاتمة. (عيني، فتح) رابه: بتشديد الموحدة وهو الذي رباه. (عيني) وأمه إلخ قيد بموتها؛ لأنها إذا كانت حية، فالمطالبة لها، وعند وجود المقذوف ليس لأحد المطالبة حتى إذا كان المقذوف حياً غائباً ليس لأحد أن يؤاخذه بالحد، وقوله: "فطلب الوالد أو الولد أو ولده حد؛ لأنه قذف محصنة بعد موتها ولهؤلاء مطالبة، والحاصل أن قذف ميتاً محصناً من فلأصله وإن علا، أو فرعه وإن سفل مطلقاً المطالبة به، والتقييد بالوالد اتفاقي، إذ حق المطالبة لأم المقذوف أيضاً، وكذا لو كان الطالب للحد غير محصن كما لو كان أصل الميت المحصن أو فرعه كافراً أو عبداً فله أن يطالب بالحد خلافاً لزفر، وعن محمد: لا تثبت المطالبة لولد البنت، ويجوز الطلب لولد الولد مع قيام الولد خلافاً لزفر.
If he says to another: 'You are not your father's' or 'You are not the son of so-and-so' in anger, he is subject to hadd; otherwise not, meaning in a state of anger and quarrel. In anger: meaning if someone says to another: 'You are not your father's' or 'You are not the son of so-and-so' referring to his known father, then if this statement is made in anger, the speaker is subject to hadd if the mother is a free Muslim; because if he is not from the father he is ascribed to, then he must be from zina by necessity. The condition of anger is because in non-anger, it might be meant as reproach meaning 'you do not resemble your father in manliness and generosity', so he is not subject to hadd due to the probability. In anger, it is meant as reality. Similarly, if he says: 'You are the son of so-and-so' not his father, he is subject to hadd if in a state of quarreling; in a state of contentment, no hadd. The analogy is that it should not be qadhf in all cases due to probability; the basis of istihsan is what is narrated from Ibn Mas'ud: 'There is no hadd except in qadhf of a muhsana or denial of a man from his father.' If he denies him from his mother by saying 'You are not your mother's', hadd is not obligatory because it is true, as lineage is only through fathers. (Fath, Mustakhlas). 'Not': meaning in a state without anger, he is not subject to hadd. ('Ayni). And [examples of non-hadd include] his denial from his grandfather, and his statement to an Arab: 'O Nabati' and 'O son of rainwater', and his attributing him to his paternal uncle, maternal uncle, or guardian, and if he says: 'O son of a fornicatress' while her mother is [dead]. Denial from his grandfather: [as he is not subject to hadd in the denial of the speaker to another] ('Ayni). An example of non-hadd: as he is not subject to hadd in the qadhif's denial of the maqdhuf from his grandfather by saying: 'You are not the son of so-and-so' while so-and-so is his grandfather, he is not subject to hadd because he is truthful in his speech, for the maqdhuf is the son of his father, not his grandfather. Similarly, if he attributes him to his grandfather, he is also not subject to hadd because it is a metaphorical attribution. Allah said: 'just as He brought your parents from Jannah' (7:27) (Mustakhlas, 'Ayni). And his statement to an Arab etc.: inflected by jar, conjoined to his statement 'denial from his grandfather', meaning just as he is not subject to hadd in his statement to an Arab. The Arabs are the well-known people, and whoever dwells in the lands of the Arabs and its peninsula and speaks with their tongue is an Arab. That is, if a man says to an Arab: 'O Nabati' (with a fatha on the ba'), in reference to the Nabataeans, who are a people in the Sawad of Iraq, a person being insulted by being attributed to them, he is not subject to hadd. According to Malik and al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and Ahmad in one narration, he is subject to hadd if he intended insult. Similarly, he is not subject to hadd in attributing him to a non-tribe. According to Malik, he is subject to hadd in every attribution by which shame afflicts him, whether Arab or non-Arab. And his statement: 'O son of rainwater', meaning also he is not subject to hadd by saying to another: 'O son of rainwater' because it is intended as a metaphor for generosity, munificence, and purity. 'Amir ibn Harithah was nicknamed 'Maa' al-Sama'' for his generosity. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/394). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He used to spend his wealth during drought like the rain. And Umm al-Mundhir was called 'Maa' al-Sama'' for her beauty and loveliness. And it was said that her children are 'Maa' al-Sama'' who were the kings of Iraq. If it is said: the state of anger precludes this intention, we say: we consider it an insult by denying bravery in anger; but as a denial that necessitates hadd, it is not, because such use is not known for that intention. (Fath). And his attributing him to his paternal uncle is another example of non-hadd. That is, he is not subject to hadd in the qadhif's attributing him to his paternal uncle, maternal uncle, or guardian because one is attributed to them metaphorically, customarily. Similarly, if he attributes him to his grandfather. Allah said, relating from Israeel and his sons: 'They said: We worship your God and the God of your fathers Ibrahim, Isma'il, and Ishaq' (2:133). Ibrahim was his grandfather, Ishaq his father, and Isma'il his uncle. And the Prophet said: 'The maternal uncle is a father.' And Allah said, relating from Nuh: 'Indeed my son is from my family' (11:45). It is said he was his wife's son. And Ashhab said: He is subject to hadd in attributing him to these during quarreling. ('Ayni, Fath). 'Rabba': with tashdid on the ba', meaning the one who raised him. ('Ayni). And his mother etc.: restricted to her death because if she is alive, the claim for hadd is hers. And when the maqdhuf is alive, no one has the right to claim, so if the maqdhuf is alive but absent, no one may prosecute for hadd. And his statement: 'Then the father asks for hadd, or the child, or his child, he is subject to hadd, because it is qadhf of a muhsana after her death, and these have the right to demand. The conclusion is that qadhf of a dead muhsan, whether for his ascendant no matter how high, or his descendant no matter how low, absolutely, the right to demand is for them. The restriction to father is coincidental, for the right to demand is also for the mother of the maqdhuf. Similarly, if the person demanding hadd is not muhsan, for example if the ascendant of the dead muhsan or his descendant is a disbeliever or a slave, he has the right to demand hadd, contrary to Zufar. According to Muhammad, the right to demand does not establish for the grandson via daughter; it is permissible for the son's son to demand even if the son is alive, contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings on what specific utterances constitute qadhf (slander) warranting hadd, and what do not. The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states the ruling, and the commentary (from Fath al-Qadir, al-'Ayni, al-Mustakhlas) elaborates. The first case: if someone says to another 'You are not your father's son' (or 'you are not the son of so-and-so') in a state of anger, then hadd applies if the mother is free and Muslim, because this implies the child is from zina. The anger condition is crucial: in non-anger, such a statement may be mere reproach (e.g., 'you do not resemble your father in generosity'), so hadd is not applied due to probability. The proof is a narration from Ibn Mas'ud that hadd is only for qadhf of a muhsana or denial of a man from his father. Denying someone from his mother does not incur hadd because lineage is through the father. The second set of examples: denying lineage from a grandfather does not incur hadd because it is truthful (everyone is a descendant of many grandfathers). Calling an Arab 'Nabati' (a non-Arab people) or 'son of rainwater' is not qadhf because these can be metaphorical (e.g., generosity). Here the text notes an inter-madhhab dispute: Malik, Shafi'i (in one view), and Ahmad hold that if the speaker intended insult, hadd applies; the Hanafi school does not apply hadd because the words are not clearly qadhf. Attributing someone to an uncle, maternal uncle, or guardian is also not qadhf because it is metaphorical; the Qur'an uses such attribution. The final part deals with slandering a deceased person: hadd applies if the deceased was muhsan, and the right to demand hadd belongs to the heirs (father, son, etc.), even if they themselves are not muhsan (e.g., a slave or disbeliever). There is an ikhtilaf with Zufar and Muhammad regarding the right of the grandson via daughter.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): Accusing a free adult Muslim of zina, or denying his lineage from his father, which incurs hadd.
المقذوف (al-maqdhuf): The person who is slandered (accused of zina).
محصنة (muhsana): A woman who is muhsan (free, Muslim, adult, chaste) in the context of qadhf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone says to an Arab 'Ya Nabati' in anger, is hadd imposed?
A) Yes, because it is an insult
B) No, because it is not a clear accusation of zina
C) Yes, only if the accuser intended it as qadhf
D) No, because the accuser must be muhsan

Key Takeaway
Hadd for qadhf is not triggered by every insult; only clear accusations of zina or denial of paternity in anger incur the penalty, and metaphorical statements are exempt.

Hadd for Qadhf: When Indirect Insults Are Not Punishable
Rulings on statements that do not incur hadd for qadhf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish statements that incur hadd for qadhf from those that do not, state the Hanafi position on metaphorical lineage attributions, and evaluate the inter-madhhab disagreement on the role of intention.

Core Idea
Only a direct attack on a person's mother's chastity or father's lineage incurs hadd; metaphorical or ethnic insults do not trigger it.

The hadd punishment for false accusation (qadhf) is severe, so the scholars carefully defined its boundaries. In this lesson, we examine several scenarios from Kanz al-Daqa'iq where no hadd is applied despite offensive speech. These cases highlight the Hanafi principle that the wording must be a clear, conventional attack on lineage. We also explore the views of other schools that consider the speaker's intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statements in question
List the specific phrases discussed: denying someone's grandfather, calling an Arab 'O Nabati', calling someone 'son of water of the sky', attributing to a relative or foster father.
2
State the Hanafi position
For each statement, note that Hanafi school does not apply hadd because the statement is either literally true (denial of grandfather) or metaphorical (attribution to uncle) or not conventionally a lineage attack (Nabati).
Key: hadd requires a direct accusation of zina against a particular person.
3
Examine the opposing views
Maliki, Shafi'i (in one view), and Hanbali (in one narration) hold that if the speaker intended insult, hadd should apply.
Note that Imam Ahmad has another narration agreeing with Hanafi.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that intention alone does not change the classification; the expression must be customarily understood as slander. Custom (urf) dictates meaning.
Refer to Quranic evidence: Allah uses 'parents' for Adam and Eve, showing metaphorical lineage allows attribution.
5
Determine the practical difference
In Hanafi practice, no hadd is applied for these cases; in other schools, if the speaker's intent is proven, hadd may be applied.
This illustrates the principle: 'Al-umur bi-maqasidiha' but limited by customary meaning.

Source Text

(فتح) و نسبته إلى عمه مثال آخر لعدم الحد أي لا يحد في نسبة القاذف إلى عمه ه أو خاله أو مربيه؛ لأنه ينسب إليهم عادة مجازاً، وكذا إذا نسبه إلى جده، قال تعالى حكاية عن إسرائيل وبنيه: قَالُوا نَعْبُدُ إِلَهَكَ وَإِلَهَ آبَائِكَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَإِسْمَاعِيلَ وَإِسْحَاقَ (البقرة:١٣٣) وإبراهيم كان جده، وإسحاق أباه وإسماعيل عمه وقال عليه: "الخال أب"، وقال تعالى: حكاية عن نوح عليه : وإنَّ ابْنِي مِنْ أَهْلِي (هود: ٤٥) قيل: إنه كان ابن امرأته، وقال أشهب: يحد في نسبته إلى هؤلاء عند المشاتمة. (عيني، فتح) رابه: بتشديد الموحدة وهو الذي رباه. (عيني)
Like his denial of his grandfather, and his saying to an Arab: 'O Nabati' and 'O son of water of the sky' and his attribution to his uncle or maternal uncle or foster father, meaning attributed to the Arabs while his mother is not his [the claimant's] mother. His denial of his grandfather: [i.e., just as he is not hadded in the denial of the speaker for another. (Ayni)] An example of non-hadd: just as the slanderer is not hadded in denying the slandered from his grandfather by saying: 'You are not the son of so-and-so' while so-and-so is his grandfather, he is not hadded because he is truthful in his speech, since the slandered is the son of his father, not the son of his grandfather. Similarly, if he attributes him to his grandfather, he is also not hadded because he attributes him metaphorically: Allah Almighty said: 'As He brought your parents out of Paradise' (al-A'raf: 27). (Mukhtasar, Ayni) And his saying 'to an Arab' etc.: in the genitive case, coordinated with his saying 'like his denial' i.e., just as he is not hadded in his saying to an Arab – and the Arabs are the known race, and everyone who dwells in the lands of the Arabs and its peninsula and speaks with the tongue of its people is an Arab – meaning whoever says to an Arab 'O Nabati' (with fatha on the ba) in attribution to the Nabat, who are a race of people in the Sawad of Iraq, who are blamed by reference to them, he is not hadded. And according to Malik and al-Shafi'i in one view, and Ahmad in one narration, he is hadded if he intended insult. Similarly, he is not hadded in attributing him to a non-tribe. And from Malik: he is hadded in every attribution that brings disgrace, whether Arab or non-Arab. And his saying 'O son of water of the sky': i.e., just as he is not hadded by his saying to another 'O son of water of the sky' because it is intended as a metaphor for generosity, magnanimity and purity, and Amir ibn Haritha was nicknamed 'water of the sky' for his generosity (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 1/394). And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: He used to provide his wealth during drought instead of rain. And Umm al-Mundhir was called 'water of the sky' for her beauty and elegance. It is said: For her children are 'water of the sky' and they were the kings of Iraq. If it is said: The state of anger prevents this intention, we say: We make it an insult for denying bravery in anger; as for it being a denial that obligates hadd, no, because its usage for that intention is not known. (Fath) And his attributing to his uncle is another example of non-hadd: i.e., he is not hadded in the slanderer's attribution to his uncle or maternal uncle or foster father, because he attribtes to them customarily metaphorically. Similarly, if he attributes him to his grandfather. The Almighty said, quoting Israel and his sons: 'They said: We worship your God and the God of your fathers, Ibrahim, Isma'il, and Ishaq' (al-Baqara: 133). Ibrahim was his grandfather, Ishaq his father, and Isma'il his uncle. And the Prophet [SAW] said: 'The maternal uncle is a father.' And the Almighty said, quoting Nuh [AS]: 'Indeed my son is of my family' (Hud: 45). It is said: He was the son of his wife. And Ashhab said: He is hadded in attributing to these [relatives] during quarreling. (Ayni, Fath) 'Rabbahu' (his foster father): with shadda on the ba, and he is the one who raised him. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, specifically on the chapter of Qadhf (false accusation of adultery). The author explains several scenarios in which the hadd penalty for qadhf (80 lashes) is not applied. The common theme is that the statement does not directly and literally attack the lineage of the slandered person. The first case: if someone says 'You are not the son of so-and-so' where so-and-so is the person's grandfather, this is literally true because a person is the son of his father, not his grandfather. Hence no hadd, even though it may be offensive. Similarly, calling an Arab 'O Nabati' (referring to the Nabataean people of Iraq) is not considered slander because it is an ethnic attribution, not an attack on lineage. However, Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i in one view, and Imam Ahmad in one narration, say hadd applies if the speaker intended insult. The author notes that according to the Hanafi school, no hadd is applied because the phrase is not conventionally used as slander. Next, calling someone 'son of water of the sky' is a metaphorical compliment meaning generous, so no hadd. In anger, it might be a sarcastic insult, but customary usage does not treat it as slander. Finally, attributing someone to an uncle, maternal uncle, or foster father is not slander because such attributions are common in Arabic metaphorical speech, as supported by Quranic verses where Ibrahim is called 'father' though he was a grandfather. The passage concludes by defining 'rabbahu' as a foster father who raised the person. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that only a clear, direct, and intentional attack on a person's mother's chastity or father's lineage incurs hadd.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): The crime of falsely accusing a married, free, Muslim person of adultery or fornication
حد (hadd): The fixed corporal punishment for certain crimes, including 80 lashes for qadhf
نبطي (nabati): A person of Nabataean origin, a non-Arab ethnic group from Iraq

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, why is there no hadd for calling an Arab 'Ya Nabati'?
A) Because the Nabateans are a noble tribe.
B) Because it is not a direct attack on lineage and is not conventionally used as slander.
C) Because the hadd for qadhf is only for free Muslims.
D) Because the speaker must be a Nabatean himself.

Key Takeaway
Only a clear, direct accusation of zina or denial of paternity incurs hadd; metaphorical, ethnic, or obviously true statements do not.

Qadhf of a Deceased Person: Standing to Demand Hadd
Ruling on standing to demand hadd for qadhf of deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on who can demand hadd for qadhf of a deceased muhsan and compare it to the Shafi'i view, as well as distinguish intra-Hanafi opinions of Zufar and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the right to demand hadd for qadhf of a deceased person extends to all ascendants and descendants, regardless of faith or status.

This lesson explores the rules for demanding hadd when the slandered person has died. The Hanafi school holds that the right is not strictly inherited but belongs to anyone whose lineage is attacked. We will examine the reasoning, the intra-Hanafi disagreements, and the contrast with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the Hanafi position: who can demand hadd after the victim's death? Focus on the general rule: any origin (asl) or branch (far') regardless of faith or slave status.
The restriction to 'father' in the text is incidental; the mother also has the right.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar denies the right to non-Muslims and slaves. Imam Muhammad denies it to a daughter's child but allows a son's child even when the son is alive. Understand these nuances.
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf take the broader view.
3
Understand the Shafi'i position and the underlying principle
Al-Shafi'i limits standing to heirs because he considers qadhf primarily a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd), which is inherited. The Hanafi response: the hadd cannot be forgiven, showing it is predominantly a right of Allah.
This difference in usul al-fiqh (haqq Allah vs haqq al-'abd) drives the ruling.
4
Apply the principle to new cases
Given the Hanafi principle, test scenarios: a non-Muslim son, a slave grandson, a daughter's child. Determine eligibility based on lineage attack.
The disgrace attaches to the lineage, not inheritance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمه إلخ قيد بموتها؛ لأنها إذا كانت حية، فالمطالبة لها، وعند وجود المقذوف ليس لأحد المطالبة حتى إذا كان المقذوف حياً غائباً ليس لأحد أن يؤاخذه بالحد، وقوله: "فطلب الوالد أو الولد أو ولده حد؛ لأنه قذف محصنة بعد موتها ولهؤلاء مطالبة، والحاصل أن قذف ميتاً محصناً من فلأصله وإن علا، أو فرعه وإن سفل مطلقاً المطالبة به، والتقييد بالوالد اتفاقي، إذ حق المطالبة لأم المقذوف أيضاً، وكذا لو كان الطالب للحد غير محصن كما لو كان أصل الميت المحصن أو فرعه كافراً أو عبداً فله أن يطالب بالحد خلافاً لزفر، وعن محمد: لا تثبت المطالبة لولد البنت، ويجوز الطلب لولد الولد مع قيام الولد خلافاً لزفر. - بني ميت، فطلب الوالد أو الولد أو ولده حدّ ولا يطالب ولد أباه، وعبد سيده بقذف أمه، ويبطل بموت المقذوف . أي حد القذف = وعند الشافعي: لا يثبت هذا الحق إلا للوارث حتى لا يكون لابنه الكافر والعبد وأولاد بنته المطالبة، وهذا مبني على أن الغالب في القذف حق العبد عنده فيورث وعندنا الغالب حق الله تعالى فلا يورث فيجوز المطالبة لمن يقع القدح في نسبه بقذفه، وهو الأصل والفرع؛ لأن العار يلحقهم لمكان الجزئية. فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يكون لهم ولاية المطالبة حياً كان المقذوف أو ميتاً حاضراً كان أو غائباً وكذا إذا مات بعد القذف، قلنا: العار يلحق المقذوف قصداً وهؤلاء يلحقهم ضمناً فلا تعتبر خصومتهم ما دام حيا؛ لأن ما ثبت في ضمن غيره لا يعطى له حكم نفسه. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
And his mother etc. is conditioned by her death, because if she is alive, the claim is hers. And when the slandered person exists, no one else has a claim; even if the slandered person is alive but absent, no one can demand hadd. And his saying: 'Then the father or the son or his child demands hadd' because it is a slander of a muhsana after her death, and these have a claim. The conclusion: Slandering a deceased muhsan (chaste person) – its claim belongs to his origin (ancestors) however high, or his branch (descendants) however low absolutely. The restriction to the father is incidental (ittifaqi), because the mother of the slandered also has a right of claim. Similarly, if the one demanding hadd is not muhsan, like if the origin of the deceased muhsan or his branch is a disbeliever or a slave, he may demand hadd, contrary to Zufar. And from Muhammad: The claim is not established for the daughter's child, but the claim is permissible for the son's child even when the son exists, contrary to Zufar. (Some text: 'of a dead person, then the father or son or his child demands hadd, and the child does not demand his father, nor the slave his master for slandering his mother, and it is invalidated by the death of the slandered' – meaning hadd of qadhf.) And according to al-Shafi'i: This right is only established for the heir, so that his disbelieving son, slave, and daughter's children do not have a claim. This is based on the principle that according to him the predominant (ghalib) in qadhf is the right of the servant (haqq al-'abd), so it is inherited; while according to us, the predominant is the right of Allah, so it is not inherited, so claim is allowed for those whose lineage is attacked by the slander, namely the origin and branch, because the disgrace reaches them due to their being part (juz'iyya) of him. If it is said: They should have the right to demand (wilayat al-mutalaba) whether the slandered is alive or dead, present or absent, and also if he dies after the slander. We say: The disgrace falls directly on the slandered (maqsudan), and these others are affected indirectly (dhimnan), so their claim is not considered as long as he is alive, because what is established within something else (thabata fi dhimni ghayrihi) is not given the same independent ruling. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage clarifies the rules for demanding hadd when the slandered person (the victim) is deceased. According to the Hanafi school, if the victim was a muhsan (free, Muslim, chaste, married) and dies, the right to demand hadd passes to the victim's 'origin' (parents, grandparents regardless of faith or status) and 'branch' (children and grandchildren, even if non-Muslim or slave). This includes the mother of the deceased. Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagreed on some points, holding that non-Muslims or slaves cannot demand, but the majority Hanafi position is that they can, because the disgrace attaches to the lineage (nasab). Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani reportedly held that a daughter's child cannot demand, but a son's child can even when the son is alive, again differing with Zufar. The passage also contrasts the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i view: al-Shafi'i holds that the right to demand hadd is inherited, so only heirs (as per inheritance law) have it, and thus a non-Muslim son or a slave cannot demand. The underlying principle is whether qadhf is primarily a right of Allah (haqq Allah) or a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd). In Hanafi, the majority right is of Allah, so the right is not inherited; rather, anyone whose lineage is attacked (namely the victim's ascendants and descendants) can demand. The text then addresses an objection: if the above is true, why can't those relatives demand while the victim is alive? The answer: the disgrace falls directly on the victim, and relatives are only indirectly affected, so their claim is subsidiary and does not override the victim's primary right.

Key Terms
محصنة (muhsana): A free, Muslim, chaste woman who has been married (consummated); also used for a man (muhsan) with similar conditions
أصل (asl): Ascendant (parent, grandparent, etc.) of the deceased
فرع (far'): Descendant (child, grandchild, etc.) of the deceased

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following can demand hadd for qadhf of a deceased muhsan?
A) Only the inheritors as per fara'id
B) The father, mother, and all children regardless of faith or status
C) Only the children who are Muslims and free
D) Only the spouse of the deceased

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, standing to demand hadd for qadhf of a deceased person is based on lineage attack, not inheritance, and thus includes all ascendants and descendants regardless of faith or legal status.

Child Cannot Demand Hadd Against Father for Qadhf of Mother
Ruling that a child cannot demand hadd for qadhf of his mother against the father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a child's right to demand hadd for qadhf against the father, identify the hadith and qiyas basis, and compare the Maliki exception.

Core Idea
A child cannot demand hadd against his father for slandering his mother because qisas is dropped between parent and child.

In this passage, the author addresses a specific limitation on the application of hadd for qadhf: when the slanderer is the father of the person whose mother is slandered. The ruling is based on a fundamental principle that certain severe punishments (qisas) are not applied between close relatives, as established by a prophetic hadith. By analogy (qiyas), the fixed hadd for qadhf is also dropped, though ta'zir (discretionary punishment) remains possible. We will examine the textual basis, the reasoning, and the contrasting view of Imam Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The ruling involves a father slandering his child's mother (the father's wife or ex-wife). The child (descendant) seeks to have the hadd punishment applied to the father. We must determine who has standing to demand hadd.
The child may be a son or daughter, any age, free or slave, Muslim or non-Muslim.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school rules: the child does not demand hadd against the father for this qadhf. The hadd is not obligatory and is not applied, but ta'zir may be imposed.
The text explicitly says 'la yutalibu' – he does not demand.
3
Trace the legal analogy (qiyas) to qisas
The author draws an analogy from the rule in qisas (retaliation) where a parent is not executed for killing their child (nor a master for killing their slave), based on the hadith 'La yuqadu al-walad bi waladihi wa la al-sayyid bi 'abdihi'. Since hadd is a fixed punishment, but less certain in its application (due to possibility of doubts like shubha), it is even more deserving of being dropped than qisas.
The analogy is from a stronger case (qisas) to a weaker one (hadd).
4
Note the exception and the role of ta'zir
The dropping of hadd does not negate ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The judge may still punish the father for slander if he sees fit. Also, Imam Malik in one narration allows the child to demand hadd, but the Hanafi consensus is against that.
The Maliki position is mentioned as an exception but not adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ميت: محصنة أو قال: يا ابن الزاني وأبوه ميت (ط) الوالد : أي والد الأم وهو جد المقذوف وإن علا ولو كافراً أو عبداً. (ط) الولد: سواء كان ابنا أو بنتا كافرا أو مسلما حرا أو عبدا عيني) ولده: أي ولد الولد وإن سفل له حق المطالبة مع بقاء الولد. (ط) حد أي القاذف؛ لأنه قذف محصنة بعد موتها وهؤلاء مطالبة لوقوع القدح في نسبهم بقذفها. (ط، عيني) ولا يطالب ولد أباه أي لا يطالب فرع وإن سفل "أباه" أي أصله ذكراً كان أو أنثى، فدخلت أمه وجدته وإن وكذا جده وإن علا، والمنفي وجوب الحد فلا ينافي لزوم التعزير وقوله بقذف أمه أي لا يطالب الفرع أباه بقذف أمه ولا العبد مولاه بقذف أمه؛ لأنهما لا يعاقبان بسببهما حتى سقط القصاص لو قتل الأب ولده والمولى عبده، وهذا بالإجماع إلا في رواية عن مالك يطالب الولد أباه بقذف ،أمه ولو كان لها ابن من غيره له أن يطالبه لتحقق السبب، وانعدام المانع وجه سقوط القصاص بالقتل قوله عليها: "لا يقاد الولد بولده، ولا السيد ه ، فالحد أولى بالسقوط؛ لعدم التيقن بسببه. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) أمه: أي أم الولد والعبد الحرة المسلمة المحصنة. (معدن)
Dead: a muhsana, or he said: 'O son of the adulterer' while his father is dead (T) 'al-walid': meaning the mother's father, i.e., the maternal grandfather of the slandered, however high, even if he is a disbeliever or slave. (T) 'al-walad': whether a son or daughter, disbeliever or Muslim, free or slave. (Ayni) 'waladuhu': i.e., the child of the child, however low, has a right to claim while the child (walad) is alive. (T) 'hadd': i.e., the slanderer's hadd, because he slandered a muhsana after her death, and these have a claim due to the disgrace falling on their lineage by her slander. (T, Ayni) 'And the child does not demand his father': i.e., a descendant, however low, does not demand his 'father' meaning his ancestor, whether male or female; so includes his mother and grandmother, however high, and similarly his grandfather however high. What is negated is the obligation of hadd; this does not preclude the necessity of ta'zir (discretionary punishment). And his saying 'by slandering his mother': i.e., the descendant does not demand the father for slandering his mother, nor does the slave demand his master for slandering his mother, because they are not punished on account of each other, to the extent that qisas (retaliation) is dropped if a father kills his child or a master kills his slave. This is by consensus, except in one narration from Malik, that the child demands the father for slandering his mother, and if she has a son from another (husband), he may demand him, because the cause is established and the impediment is absent. But the reason for dropping qisas in killing is the hadith: 'The child is not executed for the parent, nor the slave for the master.' So the hadd is more deserving of dropping due to lack of certainty in its cause. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) 'Ummihi' (his mother): meaning the mother of the child and the slave, a free Muslim muhsana. (Ma'din)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of who can demand hadd for qadhf. It first clarifies the definitions from commentary (T) and (Ayni): 'al-walid' here means the maternal grandfather of the deceased (or the father) — actually the text says 'walid al-umm' i.e., mother's father. 'Walad' includes sons and daughters regardless of status. 'Waladuhu' means grandchildren. Then it states a key ruling: a descendant (child) cannot demand hadd against his ascendant (father) for the ascendant's slander of the descendant's mother. Similarly, a slave cannot demand hadd against his master for slandering the slave's mother. The reason given is that the father/child and master/slave are not liable for capital punishment (qisas) in case of murder, so even more so, the hadd which is less certain (due to possibility of ambiguity) is dropped. The hadith cited: 'The child is not executed for the parent, nor the slave for the master.' However, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) may still be applied. There is an exception from Imam Malik: he holds that a child can demand hadd against his father for slandering his mother. But the Hanafi position (supported by consensus) is that the hadd is not applied because the relationship prevents it.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment set by the judge for crimes that do not have a fixed hadd or for which hadd is not applied for some reason
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment for murder or bodily harm, equal to the crime

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that a child cannot demand hadd for qadhf against his father for slandering the child's mother?
A) Because the father is immune from penalty
B) Because the child is not muhsan
C) Because the hadd, being a fixed punishment, is dropped when there is a relationship preventing qisas, by analogy
D) Because the mother can demand herself

Key Takeaway
The child cannot demand hadd against his father for qadhf of his mother; the ruling is based on the hadith on qisas and an analogy (qiyas) that hadd, being less certain, also drops.

Hadd for Qadhf is Void if Victim Dies: Hanafi Position
Ruling that the hadd penalty is void if the slandered person dies before execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the lapse of hadd for qadhf upon the victim's death and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by analyzing the predominant right (haqq al-shar' vs. haqq al-'abd).

Core Idea
The hadd for qadhf lapses with the victim's death because it is predominantly a divine right, not inherited.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute: whether the hadd penalty for false accusation (qadhf) is void if the victim dies before punishment. The Hanafi school holds it is void, while the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools maintain it continues. The divergence stems from a foundational principle regarding the nature of the punishment as a hybrid of divine and individual rights.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: the hadd for qadhf becomes void (batal) if the slandered person dies before the punishment is inflicted. Note that this applies also if death occurs after partial execution.
The Arabic phrase 'ويبطل بموت المقذوف' directly states this.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that the hadd does not become void; it must still be carried out after the victim's death.
Their position is based on considering the hadd as predominantly a right of the servant that is inherited.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: since the hadd contains a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd), and this right is inherited by the heirs, the punishment continues after death.
They prioritize the servant's need and the self-sufficiency of the Shar'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: the right of the servant is already protected by the state (wali), so there is no need for it to be inherited. The right of Allah (haqq al-shar') is predominant and does not pass to heirs.
The Hanafi argue that if it were purely a servant's right, the victim could forgive it before death, but forgiveness does not completely absolve – indicating divine right predominance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical difference: in Hanafi, the slanderer is not flogged if the victim dies; in other schools, flogging is still carried out unless the victim forgave before death.
This affects judicial practice and demonstrates the real-world impact of the usul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل بموت المقذوف: أي من قذف غيره فمات المقذوف بطل الحد عندنا، وقال الشافعي لا يبطل وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو مات بعد ما أقيم بعض الحد، وهذا مبني على الأصل الذي ذكرنا من أنه يورث عنده لا عندنا ولا خلاف في أن فيه حق الشرع وحق العبد، فلما اجتمع فيه الحقان، فالشافعي مال إلى تغليب حق العبد تقديماً لحق العبد؛ لحاجته وغناء الشرع، ونحن صرنا إلى تغليب حق الشرع؛ لأن حق العبد يتولاه مولاه، فيصير العبد مرعياً به؛ ولا كذلك حق الشرع؛ لأنه لا ولاية للعبد في استيفاء حق الشرع إلا نيابة، وهو أصل مشهور مختلف بيننا وبينه يبتنى عليه كثير من مسائل الفروع.
And it is void by the death of the slandered: i.e., whoever slanders another and then the slandered dies, the hadd is void according to us (Hanafis). Al-Shafi'i said it is not void, and Malik and Ahmad said he same. Based on this difference, if he dies after some of the hadd has been carried out [it is also void according to us]. This is based on the principle we mentioned: that according to him (al-Shafi'i) it is inherited, not according to us. And there is no dispute that in it (hadd) are both the right of Allah (haqq al-shar') and the right of the servant (haqq al-'abd). Since both rights are combined, al-Shafi'i leaned towards giving predominance to the right of the servant, giving priority to the right of the servant due to his need and the self-sufficiency of the shar'. But we (Hanafis) have turned to giving predominance to the right of the shar', because the right of the servant is looked after by his master (the state), so the servant is taken care of; but the right of the shar' is not like that, because the servant has no authority to execute the right of the shar' except by representation. This is a famous principle of difference between us and him, upon which many subsidiary matters are built.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the slandered person (victim of qadhf) dies before the hadd punishment is carried out. According to the Hanafi school, the hadd is void (batal) and is no longer enforced. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i, and also Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad, hold that the hadd does not become void; it must still be carried out. The same difference applies if the victim dies after part of the hadd has been executed. The underlying principle is the nature of the hadd for qadhf: it involves both a right of Allah (haqq Allah) and a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd). The Shafi'i school predominates the right of the servant, meaning that the victim's right to demand punishment continues after death and is inherited by heirs. The Hanafi school predominantly treats it as a right of Allah, which cannot be inherited; thus, when the victim dies, the right lapses. The Hanafi argument is that the right of the servant is protected by the state (the master/wali) anyway, so there is no need to preserve it after death; the right of Allah, however, is not subject to inheritance. This principle (al-asl al-mashhur) has many applications in subsidiary rulings.

Key Terms
حق الشرع (haqq al-shar'): The right of Allah (divine right) in the punishment, representing the public interest
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): The right of the individual (the victim) in the punishment, representing personal interest
موت المقذوف (mawt al-maqdhuf): Death of the slandered person – the event that triggers the dispute over the hadd's continuation
تغليب (taghlib): Giving predominance to one right over another when both are present

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the hadd for qadhf if the victim dies before the punishment is executed?
A) The hadd must still be carried out because the victim's right is inherited.
B) The hadd becomes void and is not enforced because it is predominantly a right of Allah.
C) The hadd is converted into a fine payable to the heirs.
D) The hadd is postponed until the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that the hadd for qadhf is void upon the victim's death rests on the foundational principle that the divine right (haqq al-shar') predominates over the personal right (haqq al-'abd) in this punishment.

Retraction of Admission to Qadhf
Ruling that confession to qadhf cannot be retracted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on retraction of admission to qadhf and explain the rationale based on the victim's right.

Core Idea
A confession to qadhf establishes the victim's right; retraction is not accepted because it nullifies that right.

This short passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a core principle of Hanafi hudud law: once a person admits to slandering another with qadhf, they cannot simply withdraw that admission. The reason is that the confession creates a right for the slandered party that cannot be undone by the confessor's unilateral retraction. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on protecting the rights of individuals (huquq al-'ibad) even in criminal matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? It is a person who confesses to qadhf (false accusation of adultery) and then later retracts that confession. The ruling given is that the retraction is not accepted.
The act is the iqrar (confession) and then ruju' (retraction).
2
Locate the rationale (talil)
The text states 'because the slandered has a right in it'. This indicates that the confession establishes a right (haqq) for the victim (al-maqdhuf). This right is a 'haqq al-'abd' (right of a person) that cannot be nullified by the confessor's withdrawal.
The talil is explicit: the victim's right is the reason.
3
Distinguish from other hadd cases
In other hudud like zina or theft, the right is primarily haqq Allah (right of God), and retraction before punishment may be accepted. In qadhf, because it involves slander harming a specific person's honor, the victim's right (haqq al-'abd) is paramount, hence retraction is not accepted.
This distinction explains the difference in rulings.
4
Apply the principle
If the victim forgives the slanderer, the hadd may be dropped, but the retraction itself does not nullify the confession. The confession remains as evidence establishing the right until the victim forgives or the hadd is executed.
Forgiveness by the victim is separate from retraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها: ما ذكره بقوله: "لا" بالرجوع أي من أقر بالقذف ثم رجع لم يقبل رجوعه؛ لأن للمقذوف فيه حق =
Among them: what he mentioned by his saying: 'No' [regarding] retraction, meaning whoever admits to qadhf then retracts, his retraction is not accepted, because the slandered has a right in it...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This short passage introduces the ruling that if a person confesses (iqrar) to having committed qadhf (false accusation of adultery), and then later retracts that confession, the retraction is not accepted. The reason given is that the slandered victim has a right (haqq) that has been established by the confession. Since the confession is a clear statement against the confessor's own interest, it creates a right for the victim that cannot be nullified by a simple withdrawal. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that in hadd cases, retraction after confession is generally not accepted once the hadd is established, to protect the victim's right. The passage ends abruptly, but the full ruling would also mention that the hadd is then carried out unless the victim forgives. This sub-rule is part of a larger set of sub-issues based on the foundational principle about the nature of haqq in qadhf.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A voluntary admission or confession of a right or crime
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction or withdrawal of a previous statement or claim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is retraction not accepted after one admits to qadhf?
A) Because the admission was made under coercion
B) Because the victim has a right that is established by the confession
C) Because the qadi has already pronounced judgment
D) Because the slanderer is under oath

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a retraction after confession to qadhf is not accepted because the confession establishes a personal right for the victim that cannot be nullified by the confessor alone.
